A. 
Project Development Objective 
1. 
Project 
development 
objective: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
The 
project 
development 
objective 
is 
to 
enhance 
the 
welfare 
of 
the 
population 
living 
in 
some 
of 
the 
poorest 
rural 
areas 
and  medium-sized 
towns 
and 
cities 
by 
improving 
the 
quality,  efficiency 
and 
sustainability 
of 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
services 
as 
outlined 
in 
the 
Government's 
National 
Water 
Action 
Plan. 
The project 
would 
achieve 
this 
objective 
by: 
(i) 
rehabilitating 
and 
improving 
operations 
of 
selected 
water and 
sewerage systems 
to 
increase service 
quality 
and 
efficiency; 
(ii) 
strengthening 
selected 
Apa 
Canals (water 
utilities) to 
improve 
their 
operational and 
financial 
sustainability; 
(iii) 
piloting 
the 
involvement 
of 
the 
local 
private 
sector 
in 
the 
above; 
and 
(iv) 
rehabilitating 
water 
and sanitation 
services 
in 
targeted 
rural 
areas. 
Pilot two 
concepts 
The 
project 
would 
target 
the 
cities 
of 
Cahul, 
Orhei 
and 
Soroca 
(see 
Annex 
2 
for 
additional information) 
and 
surrounding 
rural 
areas 
and 
pilot 
two 
key 
concepts. 
The 
first 
concept 
would 
involve 
a gradual 
transfer 
of 
responsibility 
to 
small-scale 
local 
operators 
through 
service contracts. 
The 
service contracts 
would 
be 
piloted 
for 
2 
years 
by 
each 
Utility. 
The 
impact 
of 
the 
pilots 
would 
be 
assessed 
after 2 
years 
to 
determine 
if 
they 
produced 
economies 
of 
scale, 
higher 
productivity, 
improved 
collections 
rates and 
lower 
costs. 
Apa 
Canals 
could 
then 
decide whether 
to renew 
the contracts 
or 
seek 
alternatives. 
The 
second  concept 
to 
be 
piloted 
would 
involve  Donor 
coordination 
and 
facilitate 
reaching 
the 
Millennium 
Development Goals 
(MDGs) 
currently 
being 
established 
by 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Environmental, 
Construction 
and 
Territorial 
Development 
(MECTD) 
in 
the 
most 
efficient way 
possible. 
MECTD 
has 
asked 
the 
World 
Bank  to 
establish  a 
Donor 
Coordination 
Task  Force. 
A 
number 
of 
Donors 
and 
international 
financing 
agencies 
operate 
in 
the 
sector, 
but 
do not 
exchange 
information 
in 
a 
systematic 
and 
coordinated 
manner. 
This 
lack 
of 
coordination 
has 
led 
to 
a 
duplication 
of 
work 
and 
methodologies, 
sometimes 
even 
in 
the 
same 
city. 
As 
part 
of 
project 
preparation, 
the Bank, 
with 
other Donors, undertook 
a 
comprehensive water and 
sanitation 
sector 
review to 
improve 
sector 
knowledge, 
identify 
staffing 
and 
other 
institutional 
constraints, 
review 
operating practices and 
prepare 
a 
phased 
and 
prioritized 
long-term 
sector  investment 
program 
based 
on 
least-cost 
solutions. 
The 
Ministry 
established 
the 
Donor 
Coordination 
Task 
Force 
to 
manage 
the 
overall 
Donor 
program, 
ensure 
that 
duplication 
does 
not 
take 
place, and 
facilitate reaching 
the 
MDGs 
for 
Moldova. 
This 
pilot would 
be 
a continuous 
and 
evolving 
process 
that 
would 
be 
monitored 
annually 
to 
determine 
its 
success 
in 
targeting 
those 
areas 
most 
in 
need, 
in 
exchanging 
information 
and 
in 
avoiding duplication 
and 
in 
reaching 
the 
MDGs. 
The cost 
for Moldova 
of 
meeting 
the 
MDG 
targets 
would 
be 
on 
the 
order 
of 
$220 
million 
during 
the 
2000-2015 period, 
or 
about $15 
million 
annually. 
I' 
Given 
that 
the 
sector 
is 
investing 
nowhere 
near such 
levels, 
the 
remaining 
annual 
investment 
cost 
to 
meet 
the 
MDGs 
will 
need 
to 
be higher 
still 
to 
compensate 
for 
the 
late 
start. 
2. 
Key 
performance 
indicators: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
Service 
quality: 
Percentage 
of 
water 
samples 
testing 
negative 
for 
pathogens; and 
Continuity 
of 
service 
(in 
hours 
per 
day). 
Sustainability 
of 
Services: 
Energy 
use 
per 
m3 
of 
water 
supplied; 
-2- 
Page  7
Unaccounted-for-water; 
Length 
of 
water 
pipe 
rehabilitated; 
Collection 
as 
a 
percentage 
of 
billing; 
Cash 
operating 
revenue 
as 
a 
percentage 
of 
cash 
operating expenditure 
plus 
debt 
service; 
Number 
of 
water 
and 
wastewater 
utilities 
receiving 
technical 
assistance; 
and 
Number 
of 
contracts 
signed 
with 
service contractors, 
or 
other 
private 
water 
supply 
and 
wastewater 
entrepreneurs. 
Service 
Contracts: 
Number 
of 
signed 
and 
effective 
contracts 
with 
local 
small 
scale 
private 
operators; 
Improved collection 
rates; 
and 
Reduction 
of 
public 
sector employees. 
Rural 
Areas: 
Number 
of 
villages 
with 
access 
to 
safe 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation; 
reduction 
in 
reported 
cases 
of 
water 
related 
diseases; 
and 
increase 
in 
hygienic 
practices 
i.e. 
clean 
latrine 
households, 
safe 
storage and 
handling 
of 
dnnking 
water. 
B. 
Strategic 
Context 
1. 
Sector-related 
Country 
Assistance 
Strategy 
(CAS) 
goal supported 
by 
the project: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
Document number: 
18896 
- 
MD 
Date 
of 
latest 
CAS 
discussion: 
June 
20, 
2002 
The 
CAS 
was discussed 
on 
May 
6, 
1999, 
and 
a 
Progress 
Report 
was discussed 
on 
June 
20, 
2002. 
The 
themes 
of 
the 
CAS 
frame 
the 
key 
development  challenges 
for 
Moldova 
under 
the 
goals 
of 
poverty 
alleviation; 
macro-stability 
and growth, 
private 
sector development 
and 
public 
sector 
reform. 
The 
proposed 
Pilot 
Water 
Supply 
and 
Sanitation 
project specifically 
supports the 
CAS 
priorities 
of 
greater 
focus 
on 
poverty alleviation. 
Numerous social 
surveys have 
confirmed 
that 
there 
is 
a 
great concern about 
inadequate 
supplies 
and 
unsafe 
water 
quality. 
Today,  there 
is 
among  the  general 
public, 
sector 
professionals 
and 
politicians 
an 
emerging 
awareness 
of 
the 
importance 
of 
addressing 
these 
issues, 
partly 
as 
a  result 
of 
widespread 
publicity 
by 
public 
health 
staff 
and  environmental  Non-Governmental 
Organizations 
(NGOs). 
Notably, 
officials 
from 
MECTD, 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
have 
all 
stated that 
the 
highest 
sector 
priority 
for Moldova 
is 
to 
enhance 
the 
welfare 
of 
the 
population 
living 
in 
rural 
areas 
and medium-sized towns 
by 
improving 
access 
to 
safer 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
services. 
The 
proposed 
pilot 
project 
would 
assist 
in 
alleviating poverty 
by 
improving 
the 
population's 
basic 
needs 
for 
safe 
water and 
hygienic 
sanitation 
and hence 
drastically 
improving 
their 
quality 
of 
life. 
2. 
Main 
sector 
issues 
and 
Government 
strategy: 
Country 
Background 
Moldova 
is 
a 
small, 
landlocked country 
situated 
between the 
western 
border 
of 
Ukraine 
and 
the eastern 
border 
of  Romania. 
Its 
population 
is 
estimated 
at 
4.3 
million 
people, 
of 
whom 
700,000 
live 
in 
Transnistria, 
east 
of 
the 
Nistru 
River. 
Moldova 
is 
a 
predominantly 
rural 
society:  60% 
of 
the 
population 
live 
in 
towns 
and 
villages 
of 
fewer 
than 
10,000 
people and 
approximately 
20% 
live 
in 
Chisinau, 
the 
capital. 
Out 
of 
all 
of 
the 
countries 
of 
the 
former 
Soviet 
Union, 
Moldova 
has 
been 
one 
of 
the 
most 
negatively 
affected 
and 
today, 
is 
the  poorest 
country 
in 
Europe. 
The 
collapse 
of 
the 
former 
Soviet 
Union 
accompanied 
by 
the 
disruption 
of 
trade 
and 
payments, 
the 
price 
shock 
which 
accompanied  the 
liberalization 
of 
the 
price 
of 
energy, 
conflict 
with 
Transnistria 
in 
1992, 
a 
series 
of 
natural 
disasters, 
and 
-3- 
Page  8
the Russian 
crisis 
of 
1998 have 
led 
to 
widespread 
poverty. 
This 
has 
resulted 
in 
massive 
migration, 
especially 
of 
skilled 
personnel, 
limited 
job 
opportunities 
and 
low 
incomes 
have 
led 
to 
an 
economic 
crisis 
particularly 
in 
rural 
areas 
and 
the 
agriculture 
sector. 
< 
10,000 
10 
- 
25,000 
25 
- 
50,000 
> 
50,000* 
> 
75,000** 
Total 
Major 
Sector 
Issues 
Rural 
12 
65,000 
Small cities 
26 
435,000 
Medium-sized 
5 
200,000 
cities 
1 
165,000 
Large cities 
7 
1,900,000 
51 
2,765,000 
Apa  Canal 
Services. 
Water 
and 
sewerage 
services 
are 
provided 
through 
Apa 
Canals 
in 
small  and 
medium-sized cities. 
In 
rural 
areas, 
a 
department 
under 
the 
mayor's 
office 
provides 
these 
services. 
Of 
the 
51 
Apa 
Canals 
in 
Moldova, 
7 
are 
located 
in 
Transnistria 
(see 
table 
below). 
Since January 
2000, 
when 
all 
Apa 
Canals were transferred 
to 
the 
sole 
ownership and 
control 
of 
municipalities, 
the 
provision 
of 
water and 
sewerage services 
has 
been 
the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
local 
authorities 
(municipality 
or 
mayor's 
office). 
Approximately 
65% 
of 
urban 
residents 
are 
connected 
to 
centralized water 
supply 
networks 
while 
about 53% 
of 
urban 
residents 
have 
sanitation 
connections. 
In 
small 
and medium-sized 
towns, 
the 
service 
coverage 
of 
water 
supply 
is 
45% 
and sanitation 
connections 
is 
40%. 
The 
hours 
of 
water 
supply 
in 
the 
public 
network 
are 
dramatically 
low 
in 
rural 
areas 
and 
only 
slightly 
better 
in 
medium 
sized cities. 
Hours 
of 
supply 
can 
range 
from 
4-18 
hours 
per 
day. 
However, large 
cities 
such 
as 
Chisinau 
and 
Balti 
generally 
have 
24 
hour 
service. 
The 
priorities 
are: 
(i) 
to 
prevent 
the 
systems 
from 
collapse 
by 
replacing 
key 
components; 
(ii) 
to 
build 
up 
local 
capacity 
to 
operate 
in 
a 
more 
efficient 
and 
sustainable way; and 
(iii) 
to 
increase coverage and 
quality 
to 
the 
unserved 
population. 
Distribution 
of 
population 
with 
Apa 
Canals 
I 
I 
I 
Classification 
1 
Number 
I 
of 
citv 
Deteriorating 
service. 
Since independence, 
water 
systems 
have 
deteriorated 
and 
are 
difficult 
to 
operate, 
service 
standards 
have 
dropped 
significantly 
and 
service 
is 
often 
intermittent. 
No 
significant 
investments 
have 
been 
made since independence 
in 
1991. Services 
provided 
by 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
and 
Mayors' offices 
are 
limited 
by 
the 
scarce 
financial 
resources that 
local 
authorities 
can allocate. 
The 
water and 
wastewater 
network 
is 
in 
dismal  condition, 
and 
5040% 
of 
the 
pipes 
need 
replacement. 
This 
is 
the 
case 
for 
all 
networks 
regardless 
of 
city-size and 
whether there 
is 
an 
Apa 
Canal 
or 
not. 
Leakages, 
up 
to 
60% 
in 
some 
systems, 
are 
a 
problem 
for 
all 
sizes 
of 
city, 
which 
corresponds 
to 
the 
poor 
condition 
of 
the 
network. 
The 
systems 
are 
overphased 
with 
high 
energy 
usage, 
and 
in 
many 
Apa 
Canals 
power 
costs 
constitute 
up 
to 
50% 
of 
total 
operating 
costs. 
Since the 
privatization 
of 
power 
supply 
in 
much 
of 
the country, 
power 
tariffs 
have increased 
threefold 
from 
US$0.019 
to 
0.054 
per 
kWh. 
To 
compensate 
for 
the 
higher 
energy 
costs, 
water 
utility 
operators have 
reduced 
the 
hours 
of 
water 
supply, 
which 
creates 
technical problems 
and, 
in 
addition, health 
hazards 
in 
many 
systems. 
-4- 
Page  9
PoorJinancial 
situation. 
The 
majority 
of 
Apa 
Canals 
are 
generally 
in 
a 
perpetual 
financial 
crisis 
as 
a 
result 
of 
low 
collections, 
low 
tariffs 
and 
high 
water 
losses. 
Collection 
rates 
are 
generally 
poor 
and can 
be 
as 
low 
as 
40% 
to 
60% 
of 
billed 
amounts 
actually 
collected 
from 
customers 
and 
even then 
involve 
considerable 
delays. 
The 
largest defaulters 
in 
monetary 
terms 
are 
public 
institutions and 
enterprises, 
in 
particular district heating 
companies 
which 
are 
the 
main 
suppliers 
of 
heat 
and 
hot 
water and 
among the 
largest 
consumers 
of 
water. 
Barter 
arrangements  are 
still 
used 
to 
settle 
accounts, 
particularly 
by 
industries and commercial 
enterprises 
that 
offer 
their 
products 
as 
payments. 
Because 
many 
customers 
are 
in 
arrears 
on 
their 
bills, 
it 
is 
impossible 
for 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
to pay 
their 
own 
bills. 
As 
a  result, 
many 
Apa 
Canals 
are 
in 
arrears 
to 
suppliers, 
contractors, 
government 
agencies 
(including 
for 
tax 
(VAT) 
payments) 
and 
electricity 
suppliers. 
Partial 
settlement 
of 
salary 
arrears 
by 
barter, 
where 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
distributes 
goods 
received 
from 
customers 
to 
staff 
in 
lieu 
of 
salaries 
is 
reported 
to 
be 
commonplace. 
Some 
Apa 
Canals, 
with 
support 
from 
Judets  (regions) 
and 
municipal 
councils  have 
reached 
voluntary 
agreements 
with 
major 
creditors 
such 
as 
electricity 
suppliers 
to 
reschedule 
their 
debts. 
Tarijk 
For 
houses 
with 
metered water 
consumption, 
typical monthly 
household 
water 
bills 
range 
from 
US$0.76 
to 
1.53. 
Typical tariffs 
for 
households 
vary from 
US$O.lO 
to 
0.50 
per 
m3 
for 
water 
supply 
and 
the 
same 
for 
wastewater. 
The tariffs  vary 
much 
more 
for 
budget 
institutions and 
enterprises, 
from 
US$0.26 
to 
3.03 
per 
m3 for 
water 
supply 
and 
from 
USSO.10 
to 
1.41 
per 
m3 
for 
wastewater. 
Sewered 
households 
pay 
a surcharge 
on 
the 
water 
bill. 
The 
share 
of 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
bills 
against 
the 
average 
per 
capita 
income 
in 
Moldova 
is 
comparatively 
high, 
reaching 
up 
to 
10%. 
Tariff 
setting 
is 
currently  largely 
based 
on 
whatever 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
can 
negotiate 
with 
the 
local 
industries 
and 
city 
political 
leaders 
and 
not 
necessarily 
based 
on 
economic 
principles 
of 
tariff 
setting. 
Government‘s 
strategy 
is 
to 
redress 
this 
imbalance 
(see 
Government 
Strategy 
section). 
Most 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
included 
in 
the 
proposed project, 
however, have 
already raised 
their 
tariffs 
to 
levels 
sufficient 
to 
cover operation and 
maintenance 
expenses. 
The 
priority 
is 
not to 
raise 
tariffs 
but 
rather 
to 
(i) 
enable 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
to 
collect what 
they 
presently 
bill; 
and 
(ii) 
lay down 
some 
guidelines 
for 
tariffs 
in 
order 
to 
protect 
consumers 
from 
monopolistic pricing. 
Relationship 
between 
municipalities 
and Apa 
Canals: 
In 
January 
2000, 
ownership 
of 
water 
supply 
infrastructure  and  responsibilities 
for 
water 
delivery 
were  transferred 
from 
the 
MECTD 
to 
the 
municipalities. 
An 
“independent” 
relationship 
exists 
and 
manifests 
itself 
in 
a 
very 
simple 
contractual 
agreement 
between the General 
Manager 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canal and 
the 
Mayor, 
where 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
takes 
on 
the 
responsibility 
of 
water 
supply 
to 
the population and 
the 
daily 
operations 
and 
management 
of 
the 
system. 
Municipalities 
do  not 
provide 
operating 
costs 
to 
the 
Apa 
Canals. 
The 
only 
municipal 
contribution 
to 
the 
water 
supply 
system 
is 
for 
capital 
expenditures 
if 
there 
are 
sufficient 
municipal 
resources. 
The 
Apa 
Canals 
all 
have 
Statutes. 
The 
contract between the 
municipality 
and 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
determines 
the 
salary 
and responsibilities 
of 
the General 
Manager. 
Salaries 
of 
staff 
and 
other 
current 
financial 
obligations 
have 
to 
come 
from 
the operation 
of 
the 
Utility. 
Staff 
are 
not 
considered 
employees 
of 
the 
municipal 
government 
but 
are 
paid 
salaries 
in 
accordance 
with 
public level 
salaries 
because 
the 
Utility 
is 
more 
than 
50% 
owned 
by 
the 
local 
government. 
However, the 
interdependence 
between the 
municipalities 
and 
the 
utilities 
remains 
critical. 
The municipalities 
have 
the 
final 
authority to 
approve 
and 
implement 
tariff 
increases 
recommended 
by 
the 
Utility 
and 
approved 
by 
MECTD. 
On 
the 
other 
hand, 
as 
owners 
of 
the 
public 
utilities, municipalities 
de 
facto 
become responsible 
for 
the 
debt 
of 
the 
Utilities 
and 
can 
incur 
contingent 
liabilities 
that 
are 
not 
transparent 
or 
accounted 
for 
in 
their 
budget. 
-5- 
Page  10
Apa 
Canals 
and 
municipalities 
appear 
to 
be 
doing 
their 
best 
under  very 
difficult 
circumstances. 
Municipalities 
do 
not 
interfere 
in 
the  operations 
of 
Apa 
Canals. 
Both 
aim 
to provide 
and 
improve 
services 
to 
their 
constituents/customers 
in 
an efficient 
and 
socially 
sensitive 
manner. 
The 
finances 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
for 
the 
operation and 
daily 
maintenance 
of 
the 
water 
supply 
system 
are 
completely 
separate 
from 
the 
municipal 
budget, 
and 
there 
is 
no 
undue 
interference 
except 
in 
the 
setting 
of 
the 
tariffs. 
Rural 
Areas. 
The 
water 
quality 
in 
small 
cities 
and 
rural 
areas 
is 
very 
poor. 
Most 
small cities 
rely 
on 
groundwater 
supplies, 
and 
large 
segments 
of 
the 
population, 
particularly 
in 
rural 
areas, 
rely 
on 
shared 
shallow wells. 
Groundwater contamination 
in 
the 
shallow 
wells,  caused 
by 
agricultural 
activity 
and 
in 
particular 
the 
use 
of 
fertilizers, 
is 
reportedly 
high. 
The 
proximity 
of 
shallow wells 
to 
latrines and 
animals, 
within 
what 
should 
be 
a 
restricted hygienic 
zone 
is 
a 
cause 
for 
even 
greater concern. Such 
contamination 
is 
generally 
rich 
in 
nitrates, 
posing 
the 
risk 
of 
methanoglobanemia  and 
a 
high 
risk 
of 
water-related 
infectious 
diseases. 
With 
animals 
close 
to 
houses 
and 
wells, 
high 
incidences 
of 
helminthes 
(some 
spread 
by 
faecal-oral  route, others 
by 
skin 
contact) 
are 
commonplace. 
Deep 
wells 
have 
higher 
water 
quality, 
except 
for 
the 
presence 
of 
fluoride, 
sometimes 
in 
concentrations 
high 
enough 
to 
cause 
dental 
fluorosis 
or 
even 
to 
affect 
bone 
development adversely. 
The  closure 
of 
local 
health 
facilities,  the 
lack 
of 
funds 
for 
primary 
health 
care, 
and 
the 
inability 
of 
patients 
to 
afford 
pharmaceuticals have 
led 
to 
hrther 
deterioration 
of 
the 
health situation 
and 
also 
greatly 
reduced 
the 
efficiency 
of 
public 
health monitoring, 
particularly 
in 
rural 
areas. 
The fact that 
the 
quality 
of 
medical 
service 
in 
rural 
areas 
is 
traditionally 
lower 
than 
in 
the 
large cities, and declining, 
suggests 
that 
morbidity 
statistics 
for rural 
areas 
most 
likely 
under-represent 
actual incidences 
of 
water 
related 
diseases. 
Donor's 
Activities. 
The 
largest 
city, 
Chisinau, 
is 
working 
with 
European 
Bank 
of 
Reconstruction 
Development 
(EBRD), 
and 
Balti 
is 
working 
with 
Japan, 
to 
improve 
their 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
systems. 
Technical 
work 
is 
well 
underway.  The 
Ministry 
of 
Environment 
and 
Energy, 
Denmark 
(DANCEE) 
program 
has 
finished 
its 
first 
water 
supply 
system 
in 
the 
village 
of 
Borceag. 
DANCEE 
has 
been 
active 
in 
Moldova 
since 
October 
2000, 
when 
Moldova 
was 
elected 
as 
a 
country 
of 
cooperation 
to 
invest 
in 
three 
rural 
areas. 
The 
key 
feature 
of 
DANCEE's 
projects 
has 
been 
the 
high 
degree 
of 
local 
participation 
and 
thereby 
consumer ownership. 
The communities 
are 
working 
with 
designated 
NGOs 
and sector experts 
to 
provide 
technical advice 
and 
assist 
in 
survey, design, 
procurement and construction 
supervision. 
In 
addition, 
the 
World 
Bank 
in 
coordination 
with 
Organization 
of 
Economic 
Co-operation 
and Development (OECD) undertook a 
comprehensive 
monitoring 
of 
performance 
indicators 
through 
the 
Moldova 
Apa 
Canal 
Association 
(MAC) 
covering 
42 
providers 
of 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
services. 
Even 
though a 
number 
of 
Donors 
and 
international financing 
agencies 
operate 
in 
the 
sector, 
information 
is 
not 
exchanged 
in 
a 
systematic 
and coordinated 
manner. 
This 
lack 
of 
coordination 
has 
led 
to 
a 
duplication 
of 
work 
and 
methodologies,  sometimes 
in 
the 
same 
cities. 
The 
Donor 
Coordination 
Task 
Force 
will 
improve 
donor 
coordination 
and 
ensure 
that 
duplication 
is 
avoided. 
Sector 
institutions. 
Water 
sector 
institutions 
in 
Moldova 
are 
evolving. 
The 
central 
authority 
responsible 
for 
the 
development and 
promotion 
of 
state 
policy for 
water 
supply 
utilities, 
as 
well 
as 
environment 
and 
natural 
resources 
is 
the 
MECTD. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
monitors 
water 
quality 
through 
its 
Public 
Health 
Bureaus. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
deals 
largely 
with 
the 
design 
of 
rural 
water 
schemes. 
At 
the 
national 
level, 
MAC 
represents 
Apa 
Canals. 
A 
positive 
development 
is 
the 
growing 
number 
of 
national 
environmental 
NGOs, 
now 
about 
180, 
officially 
registered 
in 
Moldova. 
In 
the 
long 
run, 
to avoid 
duplication 
of 
services, 
MECTD 
seeks 
to 
have water  services 
for 
both 
urban 
and 
rural 
fall 
under 
its 
organizational 
structure. 
MECTD 
also 
seeks 
to 
access 
more 
information 
on 
water 
quality monitoring 
and 
-6- 
Page  11
to 
work 
more 
closely 
with 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
to 
better 
inform 
the 
public 
on 
potential 
water 
quality 
problems. 
Legislation. 
Various legal 
instruments  have 
been 
drafted 
or 
enacted 
to 
address 
some 
of 
the 
major 
sectoral 
issues, 
such 
as 
the 
role 
of 
the 
public 
sector, 
institutional 
framework, 
use 
of 
and legal 
rights 
to 
water 
resources, 
protection 
and 
regulation 
of 
use, 
extraction 
and 
discharges 
into 
water 
resources, 
ownership 
and 
management 
of 
water 
supply 
and 
wastewater 
utilities, 
tariff 
setting, 
etc. 
Their 
implementation 
has 
been  irregular, 
however, 
because 
of 
the 
changing 
political 
orientations 
and 
coalitions, 
constraints 
on 
financial 
and  human 
resources 
and 
the 
slow 
and 
uncoordinated 
path 
of 
decentralization. 
MECTD 
has 
also asked the 
World 
Bank to 
assist 
with 
further 
strengthening 
the 
Public 
Services 
Law 
under 
the 
technical 
assistance 
component 
of 
the 
project 
and 
also 
to 
advise 
how 
to 
change 
the 
legal 
status and 
organization 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
to 
allow 
partial 
ownership 
by 
other 
commercial 
enterprises. 
Government 
Strategy 
National 
Water 
Action Plan. 
The 
Government 
has 
prepared a 
National 
Water 
Action 
Plan 
with 
a 
strategy 
and a 
sustainable 
plan 
for 
the 
sector. 
The 
Action 
Plan 
focuses 
on 
improving financial 
performance, 
gradually achieving 
profitability, eliminating 
barter 
payments 
for 
water 
services, 
reducing 
operating 
costs 
(the 
price 
of 
electricity 
has 
increased almost threefold), 
Donor 
coordination 
and 
most important, 
ensuring 
that 
the 
National 
Water 
Action 
Plan 
is 
in 
line 
with 
the 
Government's 
poverty reduction program 
and 
the 
Poverty Reduction 
Strategy 
Paper 
currently 
under preparation. 
Along 
with 
the 
Action 
Plan, 
MECTD 
requested 
that 
the 
Bank 
assist 
in 
drafting 
a 
Water 
Sector 
Note 
to 
address 
the 
long-term 
strategy 
for 
the 
sector 
beyond 
the 
current 
pilot 
project.  The 
Note 
would 
assess 
the 
current 
crisis 
in 
the 
sector, 
ways 
to 
enable 
Apa 
Canals 
to 
become 
financially 
viable  utilities, 
cost-effective 
investments, 
commercial practices and 
local 
private 
sector 
involvement 
and 
prioritization 
of 
water 
management 
policies. 
This 
Note 
is 
being 
produced 
with 
officials 
from MECTD, 
Apa 
Canals, 
sector specialists, 
local 
officials, 
MAC 
and a 
high 
level 
of 
public 
participation 
that includes 
NGOs, 
rural 
water 
communities 
and stakeholders 
that 
are 
active 
in 
both 
the 
urban 
and 
rural 
water 
sectors. 
The 
Note 
would 
build 
on 
the 
findings 
of 
the 
National 
Action 
Plan. 
Creating 
the 
Conditions 
for 
Financial 
Autonomy. 
The 
Government 
is 
focusing 
on 
rehabilitated physical 
infrastructure,  gradually 
increased 
investments 
and 
professional 
staff 
in 
the 
utilities. 
This 
will 
enable 
utilities 
to 
provide 
better 
service and 
to 
raise 
the 
level 
of 
tariffs 
to 
cover operation 
and 
maintenance. 
The 
Government 
is 
committed 
to 
the 
creation 
of 
modern 
tariff 
regulation, 
in 
which 
tariffs 
are 
cost-based 
and 
predictable. 
Because 
the 
scope 
for 
raising tariffs 
is 
limited 
by 
the 
population's income 
levels, 
lifeline 
tariffs 
are 
provided 
by 
the 
Apa 
Canals, 
in 
which 
a 
minimum 
amount 
of 
water 
is 
provided 
at a 
subsidized 
tariff. 
The 
large 
cross-subsidies 
from 
industries 
to 
domestic consumers 
are 
gradually 
being 
phased 
out 
but 
since 
tariffs 
for 
public 
institutional 
and 
commercial 
enterprises 
are 
up 
to 
10 
times 
the 
average 
tariff for 
domestic 
consumers, 
this 
can 
only 
be 
done 
gradually. 
The 
Apa 
Canals 
are 
moving 
closer 
to 
end 
the 
cross-subsidy 
between 
industry 
and domestic customers. 
MAC 
will 
assist 
the 
individual 
municipalities 
in 
deciding 
on 
the 
tariff 
through 
the 
provision 
of 
benchmarking indicators 
that 
they 
publish 
annually. 
Municipalities 
will 
be 
able 
to 
compare 
their 
own 
tariff 
levels 
and cost 
levels 
with 
other 
municipalities 
and 
judge 
to 
what 
degrees 
tariff 
increases 
are 
justified. 
With 
gradually 
increased 
hours 
of 
service 
per 
day 
and 
with 
extended and 
more 
precise 
metering, 
individual Apa 
Canals 
will 
also 
gain 
more reliable 
consumption 
and 
billing 
data 
which 
will 
make 
it 
possible 
to 
analyze 
who 
is 
subsidizing 
and 
who 
is 
receiving 
subsidies. 
Such 
data 
will 
be 
valuable 
to 
restricting 
subsidies 
to 
the 
general 
population 
and 
-7- 
Page  12
gradually targeting 
them 
at 
a 
smaller 
number 
of 
better 
identified 
poor 
consumers. 
This 
will 
be 
possible 
because 
water  consumption 
rises 
with 
income 
levels: 
wealthier 
consumers consume 
more 
water 
per 
capita. 
MAC'S 
annual 
report 
on 
utility 
data 
will 
also 
include 
the 
tariff 
schedules 
for 
participating 
Apa 
Canals 
and 
MAC 
will 
also 
be 
able 
to provide 
ad hoc 
advice 
on 
the 
calculation and implementation 
of 
tariffs. 
Improved 
Donor 
Coordination 
to 
meet 
the 
MDGs. 
A 
financially 
weak 
water 
supply 
and 
wastewater 
sector depends 
on 
external 
financial 
assistance 
to 
fund 
new 
investment. 
This 
dependence 
introduces 
the 
danger, 
however, 
of 
uncoordinated external 
financial 
assistance, 
particularly 
when 
there 
is 
a 
multitude 
of 
bilateral 
programs. 
To 
reduce 
this 
risk, 
MECTD 
has 
under 
its 
National 
Action 
Plan 
asked the 
World 
Bank to 
establish and 
lead 
a 
Donor 
Coordination Task 
Force  and 
use 
this 
forum 
to 
direct 
Donor 
assistance 
to 
meet 
the 
MDGs. 
MECTD 
has 
also requested 
the 
Bank 
to 
work 
with 
other 
Donors 
on 
longer 
term 
strategy  development 
aspects, 
including 
cost  recovery, 
financial 
systems/planning, 
and 
staff 
training. 
This 
process 
would 
be 
a continuous 
and 
evolving 
process 
that 
would 
continue 
during 
project 
implementation. 
Periodic 
meetings 
of 
the 
Donors 
would 
also 
benefit 
from 
the  annual 
compilations 
of 
monitoring 
indicators that 
MAC 
would 
prepare 
under 
the 
project. 
Since 
1996, 
MAC 
has 
also 
compiled 
technical, 
financial, 
and 
managerial 
monitoring 
indicators. 
These 
represent 
a 
standardized 
and 
valuable 
database 
that 
could 
evolve 
into quality 
regulation 
for 
the 
sector. 
The 
minor 
cost 
of 
updating 
this 
database 
on 
an 
annual basis 
would 
be 
directly 
contracted 
with 
MAC 
and 
paid 
by 
the 
credit. 
3. 
Sector 
issues 
to 
be 
addressed 
by 
the 
project 
and 
strategic 
choices: 
Selection 
of 
areas 
to 
be 
included 
in 
the 
project. 
Apa 
Canals 
in 
the 
project 
were 
competitively 
selected 
on 
the 
following 
criteria: 
Apa 
Canals 
with 
major 
services problems; 
Apa 
Canals 
that 
cover 
a 
population 
of 
at 
least 
15,000; 
Apa 
Canals that 
are 
gradually 
able 
to 
move toward 
full 
cost 
recovery and 
Apa 
Canals 
with 
tariffs 
that 
are 
sufficient 
to 
cover operation and 
maintenance. 
Three out 
of 
the 
four 
cities 
- 
Cahul, 
Orhei 
and 
Soroca 
- 
have 
been 
included 
in 
the 
project. 
It 
was 
agreed 
that the 
remaining 
Apa 
Canal 
would 
be 
identified 
during 
project 
implementation. 
Rural 
areas 
targeted 
are 
those 
with 
the 
highest 
level 
of 
polluted 
drinking 
water and 
highest 
number 
of 
outbreaks 
of 
diseases. 
(See 
Village 
Water 
Supply 
under 
Technical 
Summary 
for 
additional information). 
Focus on 
operating 
efficiency. 
The 
project 
would 
assist 
in 
introducing 
appropriate 
service 
standards 
and the 
tools 
to 
reach and 
maintain 
these standards. 
Cost 
breakdowns 
for 
Apa 
Canals 
confirm 
that 
electricity 
is 
by 
far 
the 
largest 
operating 
expense 
(ranging 
from 
25% 
to 
50% 
of 
total 
annual 
operating 
costs), 
well 
ahead 
of 
maintenance 
and materials 
(15% 
to 
25%) 
and 
salaries (5% 
to 
15%). 
The project 
would 
not 
support  investments 
in 
new 
capacity 
but 
would 
focus 
instead 
on 
reduction 
of 
electricity 
consumption 
and leakage 
through 
improving 
operating  efficiency, 
rehabilitating 
and 
maintenance 
of 
assets. 
The 
investments 
in 
rehabilitation 
have 
the 
objective 
of 
reducing 
service costs 
in 
order 
to 
make 
services 
affordable 
and 
in 
order 
to 
enable 
cash 
flows 
to 
turn 
sustainable. 
Financial 
improvements. 
An 
urgent 
need  exists 
for 
improved 
cost-recovery 
mechanisms  and 
tariff-setting principles 
and 
procedures 
in 
the 
water 
supply 
and 
wastewater 
sector. 
Cost 
recovery 
will 
be 
enhanced 
by 
increasing the 
efficiency 
of 
the system 
and reducing 
the 
costs 
of 
its 
operations 
through 
technical improvement 
combined 
with 
improved 
collections 
and an 
upgraded customer 
database 
and 
ensuring 
payment 
by 
the 
large 
debtors  and 
Government 
entities. 
This  effort 
would 
require 
the 
full 
support 
of 
local 
councils and mayors 
in 
reaching 
agreements 
with 
large 
debtors 
and 
stricter 
enforcement 
of 
legal 
sanctions against defaulters. 
Tariff 
setting 
principles 
have been based 
on 
a 
formula 
to 
ensure 
that 
established 
tariff 
covers 
operation and 
maintenance. 
-8- 
Page  13
A 
program 
to 
increase 
public 
awareness 
would 
be 
started 
to 
show 
the relationship 
between 
service 
levels, 
tariffs 
and 
bill 
payment. 
Measures 
to 
increase 
collection 
rates 
would 
in 
most 
cases 
be 
of 
more 
benefit 
in 
the  short 
term 
than 
tariff 
increases 
alone 
in 
improving 
the 
financial 
situation 
of 
Apa 
Canals. 
Tariff 
increases, 
if 
not 
properly 
managed and 
combined 
with 
other 
measures 
to 
mitigate 
negative impacts 
on 
poorer 
customers, 
might 
actually lead 
to 
inequities 
and 
decreased 
collection 
rate 
if 
the 
population 
does 
not 
perceive 
tangible 
benefits. 
Therefore, 
the 
project 
would 
focus 
on 
improving 
service 
and increasing 
collections 
first, 
and then 
address 
tariff 
increases 
to ultimately 
reach 
full 
cost 
recovery. 
Summarizing, 
guidelines 
are 
needed 
for fixing 
tariffs 
that 
will 
(i) 
relate 
tariffs 
to 
cost 
levels 
of 
efficient 
operations 
so 
that 
consumers 
should not 
be 
forced 
to 
pay 
for 
inefficiencies 
of 
service providers; 
(ii) 
make 
subsidies 
explicit 
and gradually restricted 
to low-income 
consumers 
with 
the 
priority 
given 
to 
increasing 
coverage; 
and 
(iii) 
that 
will 
provide 
incentives 
for 
increasing 
collections 
in 
order 
to 
make the cash 
flows 
of 
service 
providers 
sustainable. 
The 
project 
would 
support 
improvements 
in 
financial 
reporting 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals. 
It 
has 
been 
agreed 
that 
each 
Utility 
would 
provide 
a yearly 
Financial 
Action 
Plan 
composed 
of 
annual reports, 
including 
detailed 
financial 
statements 
and 
financial 
forecasts 
to 
ensure 
that 
projected 
operating 
revenues 
are 
sufficient 
to 
meet 
operating 
and 
maintenance 
expenses 
for 
the 
next 
fiscal 
year. 
If 
these 
requirements 
cannot 
be 
met, 
the 
utilities 
would 
take 
all 
necessary 
measures, 
including 
further 
improvement 
of 
collections 
and, 
where 
needed, 
adjustments 
of 
structures 
or 
levels 
of 
rates. 
An 
important 
and 
integral part 
of 
this 
plan 
would 
be 
to 
set 
break-even 
goals and 
then 
move 
towards 
profitability 
goals 
for 
each 
Apa 
Canal. 
The 
Financial 
Action 
Plan 
for 
each 
Apa 
Canal 
has 
been 
initiated 
to 
ensure that 
Apa 
Canals 
move 
toward 
covering 
operating and 
maintenance 
costs 
in 
the 
initial 
phases 
of 
the 
project. 
In 
so 
doing, 
the 
project 
would  not  only 
assist 
in 
improving 
the 
Apa 
Canal's 
financial 
performance 
but 
also 
address 
the 
quasi-fiscal 
deficit 
problem 
currently 
caused 
by 
sector operations. 
The Financial 
Action 
Plan 
would 
be 
made 
available 
to 
the 
public 
and 
municipalities. 
Institutional 
strengthening. 
MECTD 
has asked 
the 
Bank 
to 
strengthen 
the 
institutional 
capacity 
of 
the 
sector 
in 
a 
number 
of 
ways. 
MECTD 
capacity 
would 
be 
strengthened 
as 
the 
overall 
responsible agency 
for 
setting 
policy. 
MECTD 
would 
use 
the 
project 
as 
a 
vehicle 
to 
initiate 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
National 
Water 
Action 
Plan. 
Under 
the 
Plan, 
MECTD 
will 
pursue 
gradual 
modernization 
of 
the 
sector. 
Assistance 
would 
be 
provided 
towards 
gradually 
developing 
quality 
sector 
regulation 
that 
would 
build 
on 
the comprehensive 
monitoring 
of 
performance indicators that 
MAC 
produced 
with 
assistance 
from 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
OECD. 
The  analytical 
works 
covered 
42 
providers 
of 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
services 
in 
Moldova 
from 
1996 
to 
2002. 
This 
research assisted 
in 
developing 
a better understanding 
of 
problems 
facing 
Apa 
Canals 
in 
becoming 
commercial and 
autonomous 
utilities. 
In 
addition, 
these 
works 
enable 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
of 
the 
indicators; 
assessing 
effects 
of 
investment, 
institutional 
and 
other 
transformations 
in 
the 
water 
sector; 
and 
comparing 
of 
data 
with 
findmgs 
from 
other Former Soviet 
Union 
(FSU) 
and 
OECD 
countries. 
It 
is 
proposed 
that 
through 
the 
project, 
the 
performance 
monitoring 
of 
the 
MAC 
become 
the 
cornerstone 
of 
sector 
quality 
regulation. 
Even 
though 
Apa 
Canals 
fall 
under 
the 
purview 
of 
MECTD, 
the 
sector 
is 
largely 
fragmented, 
with 
inadequate representation 
of 
Apa 
Canals 
at 
the 
national level. 
The 
project 
would 
strengthen 
MAC 
to 
better 
serve 
the 
interests 
of 
Apa 
Canals 
at 
the 
national 
level 
through 
management support, 
organizational 
and 
staff 
development  and 
training. 
MAC 
occupies 
a 
strategic 
position 
in 
the 
sector  and 
fills 
an 
important 
gap 
between 
individual Apa 
Canals and 
national 
agencies. 
-9- 
Page  14
The 
financial 
relationship 
between 
municipal 
governments and 
Apa 
Canals 
is 
at 
arm's 
length. 
The 
Apa 
Canal budget 
is 
completely 
separate 
from 
the 
municipality. 
Apa 
Canals 
may 
receive 
capital 
expenditure 
if 
resources 
allow 
or 
in 
cases 
of 
emergency. 
The 
project 
would allow for 
improvements 
in 
municipal 
understanding 
of 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
water 
sector. 
Mayors 
have 
been 
consulted 
and 
are 
committed 
to 
improve 
the 
situation 
for 
their 
constituents. 
Cooperation among different 
sectors 
and 
Ministries 
is 
essential 
to 
improve 
the 
workings 
of 
the 
water 
sector. 
Monitoring 
of 
service 
quality 
would 
have 
to 
be 
strengthened and 
is 
best 
handled 
by 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health. 
Better 
coordination 
mechanisms 
would 
have 
to 
be 
established 
between 
the 
Minister 
of 
Health 
and 
MECTD. 
The 
development 
schemes 
for 
rural 
infrastructure 
fall 
under 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
and 
it 
would 
be 
necessary 
to 
gradually 
bring 
together 
the 
development 
schemes 
and the 
implementation 
part 
that 
is 
handled 
by 
MECTD. 
Local 
Entrepreneurs. 
MECTD 
has 
asked 
the 
Bank 
to 
explore 
the 
possibility 
of 
local 
rather than 
foreign 
private 
sector 
participation.  Foreign private 
sector 
participation 
in 
the 
sector 
(perhaps 
with 
the 
exception 
of 
Chisinau 
and 
Balti) 
is 
not 
a 
viable 
option 
because 
of 
the 
lack 
of 
viable 
cash 
flows to pay 
high-cost 
foreign 
private 
operators. 
Municipal 
ownership 
would 
certainly 
continue 
in 
the 
short 
to medium 
term. 
The 
project 
would 
attempt, however, 
to 
achieve a 
gradual transfer 
of 
responsibility and 
staff 
from 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
to 
small 
local 
operators-to-be. 
The  remuneration 
of 
the 
small-scale 
local 
entrepreneurs 
would 
be 
based 
on 
a 
fixed 
amount 
of 
payment 
per 
services rendered, such 
as 
meter 
readings 
verified, 
amount 
of 
collected 
cubic 
meters 
of 
water 
from 
consumers, 
and 
cubic 
meters 
pumped. 
The 
planned 
gradual 
growth 
of 
local 
entrepreneurs 
would 
accomplish 
a 
number 
of 
strategic 
objectives: 
a 
decrease 
in 
the 
number 
of 
public 
sector 
staff 
since 
they 
would 
be 
removed 
from 
the 
payroll 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
and therefore 
cease 
being 
public 
sector employees; 
the 
retention 
of 
system 
know-how 
would 
remain 
within 
the water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
sector since 
their knowledge 
of 
the 
systems 
would 
not 
be 
lost 
since 
they 
would 
continue 
workmg 
with 
the 
systems; 
an 
increase 
in 
the 
number 
of 
local 
small-scale 
entrepreneurs 
working 
with 
greater 
efficiency 
since 
they 
would work 
under 
changed 
incentives  that 
would 
encourage 
higher 
productivity; 
and 
a 
change 
in 
incentives 
that 
could 
be 
expected 
to improve 
collection 
rates such 
as 
when 
they 
are 
paid 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
volume 
of 
water 
supplied 
and 
collected. 
Local 
entrepreneurs 
would 
be 
paid only 
if 
they 
collected 
payment 
from 
consumers 
which 
arguably 
increases 
incentives 
to 
collect. 
At 
the 
same 
time, 
higher 
payment 
would 
be 
facilitated 
if 
service 
quality 
improved; 
and 
local 
entrepreneurs 
would 
be 
expected 
to 
be 
responsible 
for 
operating 
and 
maintaining 
the 
systems 
and 
to 
pay 
for 
the 
corresponding 
costs 
which 
would 
give 
them 
an 
incentive 
for 
raising 
the 
efficiency 
of 
operations.

Different 
forms 
of 
local 
entrepreneurs 
are 
likely 
in 
the 
project 
towns. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
Cahul, 
it 
is 
likely 
that 
a group 
of 
employees 
in 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
will 
leave 
the 
Utility 
and 
form 
a company 
to 
which 
the operations 
and 
maintenance 
of 
part 
of 
the 
system 
will 
be 
outsourced. 
Their 
payment 
will 
be 
benchmarked 
against 
historical 
costs 
and 
it 
is 
expected 
that the 
incentive 
of 
higher 
remuneration 
through higher 
productivity 
will 
improve 
both 
quality 
and 
efficiency 
of 
service. 
The 
present management 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
is 
planning 
to 
make 
the 
local 
entrepreneur responsible 
for 
part 
of 
the 
distribution 
system 
and 
for 
part 
of 
the 
production 
system. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
Orhei, 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
management 
plans 
to 
outsource the maintenance 
of 
the 
production 
and 
distribution 
system 
to local 
service, 
benchmarking 
their 
remuneration 
against 
the 
present 
costs 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
for 
the 
same 
work. 
It 
is 
likely 
that 
the 
professionals 
to 
whom 
the 
maintenance 
will 
be 
outsourced 
will 
be 
ex-employees 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canal. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
Soroca, 
the 
planned 
service contract 
will 
be 
in 
the 
reconciliation 
of 
actual 
meter readings against estimated 
meter 
readings. 
This 
function 
is 
important 
since 
present 
consumption 
is 
the 
result 
of 
consumers' 
self-reporting 
that 
is 
likely 
to 
be 
inexact. 
It 
is 
likely 
that 
the 
personnel 
to 
whom 
this 
function could 
be 
outsourced 
would 
be 
groups 
of 
individual 
who 
live 
in 
the 
same 
areas 
where 
consumption 
readings 
will 
be 
validated 
against 
self-reported 
readings. 
The pre-qualification criteria 
will 
vary 
with 
the 
complexity 
of 
the 
tasks 
to 
be 
outsourced. 
For 
those 
who 
will 
operate 
and 
maintain 
sections 
of 
the 
production 
and 
distribution 
systems, 
the 
criteria 
will 
involve 
relevant professional 
experience 
with 
the 
same 
or 
similar 
tasks. 
For 
the 
validation 
of 
estimated 
meter 
readings 
the 
pre-qualification criteria 
will 
be 
simpler 
and include knowledge 
of 
the 
geographical 
areas 
where 
work 
is 
planned. 
Targeting 
of 
Rural 
Areas. 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
for 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
water 
systems 
of 
up 
to 
10 
villages. 
The 
focus 
would 
be 
on 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
existing 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
systems, 
existing wells 
and selected supplemental 
wells. 
A 
water 
hygiene 
and 
sanitation 
information 
program 
would 
be 
a 
critical 
part 
of 
the 
village 
water 
component. 
Until 
recently,  consumers 
and 
political 
leaders 
were 
relatively 
unconcerned 
with 
the 
health 
risks 
resulting 
from 
environmental 
pollution 
and 
poor 
water 
quality. 
Today, however, 
many 
stakeholders 
are 
becoming 
aware 
of 
the 
importance 
of 
addressing 
these 
issues. 
The 
rural 
component 
would 
be 
contracted 
competitively 
to 
a 
local 
NGO 
or 
a 
consortium 
of 
NGOs, 
who 
will 
manage 
the day-to-day supervision. 
I 
The water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
(WSS) 
targets under the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals 
(MDGs) 
are 
global and 
have 
not 
been 
specified 
for 
each 
developing country. 
However, 
there 
are 
two 
that 
are 
relevant for Moldova: 
Target 
# 
10 
under the 
Goal to 
Ensure 
Environmental Sustainability: 
Target 
# 
11 
as 
amended 
by 
the Johannesburg 
World 
Summit 
of 
Sustainable 
Development 
(WSSD) 
in 
August 
2002. 
Reaching 
it 
will 
also 
underpin 
the general 
goal 
of 
ensuring environmental sustainability 
to 
the extent that the water 
pollution 
diminishes: 
Quantification 
of 
the 
WSS 
Targets 
for 
Moldova 
The present 
incremental population to 
be served 
with 
WS 
will 
be 
approximately 4.3*(1.00-0.53)*0.5=1 
.O 
million, 
given 
that the 
urban 
share 
of 
the 
population 
is 
40%, 
the 
rural 
share 
60%, and 
the respective 
WS 
levels 
are 
65% and 
45%. 
Assuming 
an 
incremental 
per 
capita 
investment cost 
of 
$100 
for 
water 
supply 
the 
WS 
MDG 
cost 
is 
$100 
million 
to 
be 
expended 
in 
the 2000-2015 period. 
The present 
incremental population to 
be served 
with 
Sanitation 
will 
be 
approximately 
4.3*(1.00-0.46)*0.5=1.2 
million, 
given that 
the 
urban 
share 
of 
the 
population 
is 
40%, 
the 
rural 
share 
60%, 
and the 
respective 
sanitation levels 
are 
45% and 40%. Assuming 
an 
incremental 
per 
capita investment 
cost 
of 
$100 
for 
sanitation 
(with 
some 
allowance 
for 
wastewater treatment 
although 
rural 
areas 
will 
have 
to 
opt for latrines) the sanitation 
MDG 
cost 
is 
$120 
million 
to 
be 
expended 
in 
the 
2000-2015 period. 
It 
should 
be 
noted that Moldova's population 
decreased 
by 
0.2% 
annually 
during 
the 1990-2000 
period. However, the calculation 
of 
incremental 
MDG 
investment 
costs 
assumes an 
unchanged 
population 
since 
there 
will 
be 
some 
need 
to 
upgrade 
the 
present 
deteriorated 
systems. 
Halve 
by 
2015 
the proportion 
of 
people 
without 
sustainable 
access 
to 
safe 
drinking 
water 
Halve 
by 
2015 the 
proportion 
of 
people 
without 
adequate 
sanitation.

to

3. 
Benefits 
and 
target population: 
The 
project 
would 
benefit 
the 
population 
in 
the selected 
project 
sites 
through improvements 
in 
safe 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
services and other 
public 
health 
benefits. 
By 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project, approximately 
150,000 
people 
in 
medium-sized towns 
and 
50,000 
inhabitants 
in 
small 
rural 
towns, 
or 
a 
total 
of 
200,000, 
would 
have 
access 
to 
better water 
and 
sanitation 
services. 
In 
rural 
areas 
and 
smaller  districts, 
the 
project's 
target 
population 
would 
be 
those 
taking 
care 
of 
animals adjacent 
to 
their 
house, 
often 
in 
the 
vicinity 
of 
the shallow 
well 
used 
for 
water 
supply. 
This 
imposes 
a 
public 
health 
risk 
in 
the 
form 
of 
faecal 
contamination 
of 
poorly 
sealed 
wells, 
not 
to 
mention 
increased 
risk 
of 
worms and other 
diseases 
spread 
by 
animals 
to 
people  most  effected 
by 
such 
contamination, 
in 
most 
cases, 
the 
women 
and 
children 
responsible 
for 
water 
supply. 
Rivers 
and 
streams 
would 
be 
cleaner. 
Improvements 
in 
waterborne 
diseases 
in 
rural 
areas, 
particularly 
for 
children 
would 
improve 
the 
overall 
population's 
health through 
sufficient 
and 
safe 
water 
quality. 
Benefits 
would 
also accrue 
through appropriate 
planning 
of 
future 
development  geared 
to 
Moldova's 
needs 
and 
capacity, 
through 
the 
phased approach 
of 
medium 
and 
long-term 
investment 
plans based 
on 
appropriate technology, 
affordability 
and 
environmental 
protection. 
4. 
Institutional 
and 
implementation 
arrangements: 
On-Lending 
Arrangements. 
The 
credit 
proceeds 
would 
flow 
to 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
via 
a Development 
Credit 
Agreement 
(DCA) 
with 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance 
on 
behalf 
of 
the 
Government 
of 
Moldova 
and Subsidiary 
Credit 
Agreements 
between the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance and 
each 
Apa 
Canal. 
The 
Government 
would 
receive 
the standard 
IDA 
terms 
with 
a 
IO-year 
grace 
period, a 
final 
maturity 
of 
40 
years, 
a 
service charge 
of 
.75% 
and a commitment 
fee 
of 
SO%. 
Given 
the 
sector's characteristics 
and 
the 
financial 
position 
of 
the 
utilities, 
the 
on-lending 
terms recommended 
is 
a 
maturity period 
of 
not 
less 
than 
twenty 
(20)  years, 
a 
grace 
period 
of 
six 
(6) 
years, 
and 
an 
interest 
rate 
of 
not 
more than 
1.5% 
per 
annum. 
Institutional 
Arrangements. 
A 
project-funded 
PIU 
has 
been 
created since 
October 
2001 
with 
the 
specific 
task 
of 
implementing 
the 
project 
during 
2003-2007 
under 
the 
overall responsibility 
of 
MECTD. 
The 
PIU 
will 
be 
legally 
registered and 
report 
to 
the 
MECTD. 
The 
hlly 
staffed 
PIU 
consists 
of 
a 
Project 
Head, 
Procurement 
Officer, 
Engineer, 
Financial 
Manager, 
Accountant  and  a 
translator. 
All 
except 
the 
Translator 
have been 
hired. 
The 
PIU 
would 
not 
be a 
permanent 
part 
of 
MECTD 
but 
would 
exist 
only 
as 
long 
as 
the 
project 
is 
implemented. 
It 
would 
free 
the 
MECTD 
from 
the task 
of 
implementation, thereby 
malung 
the 
MECTD 
role 
of 
setting 
policy 
clearer. 
In 
addition,  a  Steering 
Committee 
based 
within 
MECTD, 
a 
working 
group 
of 
a 
maximum 
of 
seven 
persons 
would 
assist 
to provide 
policy 
guidance 
regarding 
Project 
implementation. 
The  PIU, 
in 
close 
coordination 
with 
the 
Apa 
Canals, 
would 
be 
responsible 
for all 
procurement, 
financial 
management 
and 
disbursement 
aspects. 
Implementation Arrangements. 
The 
project 
would 
be 
managed 
by 
the 
PIU 
in 
full 
coordination 
with 
the 
Apa 
Canals  and where 
needed, 
assisted 
by 
Technical 
Engineers/Advisors 
or 
NGOs. 
The 
Technical 
EngineerdAdvisors 
may 
assist 
with

and 
implementation 
of 
investments 
and 
improvements 
in 
network rehabilitation 
and 
operations, 
commercial 
practices and 
institutional 
strengthening. 
Under 
no 
circumstances 
are 
the 
Apa 
Canals, 
PIU 
or 
villages 
subordinate 
to 
the 
Technical 
Advisors 
under 
the 
proposed

structure. 
The 
Rural 
Component 
would 
be 
handled 
by 
a 
coordinating 
NGO 
under

would

The 
NGO

It

selected 
and

a

Water 
Working 
Group, 
including 
appointed and elected 
members. 
It 
would

for: 
(i)

the 
establishment 
and 
maintenance 
of 
effective 
billing 
and 
collection 
by 
the 
local 
water 
users 
association; 
(ii) 
auditing 
of 
the 
village 
water 
users associations 
and 
itself 
be 
subject 
to 
auditing 
to 
be 
-14- 
Page  19
submitted 
to 
the

(iii) 
monitoring 
the 
environmental,

and  financial 
operation 
of 
the

as

to

lessons 
learned 
as

Activities 
in 
each 
village 
would 
be 
designed 
to 
meet 
village-specific 
needs. 
Procurement 
Arrangements. 
A 
Procurement 
Officer 
has 
been 
hired 
and 
attended 
training along 
with 
Project 
Head to 
ensure 
that 
procurement 
is 
undertaken 
efficiently 
in 
a 
timely 
manner 
and 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Bank 
guidelines. 
The 
PKJ 
would 
have 
overall  responsibility 
for 
project 
implementation, 
including 
procurement 
actions 
in 
coordination 
with 
the 
Apa 
Canals. 
Disbursements. 
Disbursements 
from 
the 
IDA 
Credit 
will 
be 
made 
based 
on 
transaction  based 
disbursement 
methods 
(i.e., 
from 
the 
Special 
Account 
with 
reimbursements 
made 
based 
on 
Statements 
of 
Expenditures 
(SOEs) 
and 
full 
documentation, 
and 
direct 
payments 
from 
the 
Credit 
Account). 
The 
Government 
will 
establish, 
maintain 
and 
operate, 
under 
terms 
and conditions 
acceptable 
to 
the Bank, 
a 
separate 
Special 
Account 
(SA)  denominated 
in 
a 
freely 
convertible 
and 
stable 
currency. 
The 
task 
team 
and 
the 
client, 
using 
the 
Bank's 
criteria 
for 
special account banks 
will 
evaluate 
the extent 
to which local 
commercial 
banks 
meet the requirements 
and 
the 
client 
will 
select a bank, 
subject 
to 
the 
Bank's approval. 
The 
PIU 
will 
use a 
local 
project 
account 
for 
the counterpart 
contributions 
to 
the 
Project. 
Flow 
of 
Funds. 
Project 
funds 
will 
flow 
from: 
(i) 
the Bank, either 
via 
a 
single 
SA 
which 
will 
be 
replenished 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
SOEs 
or 
by 
direct 
payment 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
direct 
payment 
withdrawal 
applications; 
and 
(ii) 
the 
Government, 
via 
the Treasury 
at 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance 
(MOF) 
on 
the basis 
of 
payment 
requests 
prepared 
by 
the 
PKJ. 
The 
Government 
will 
authorize 
the 
MECTD, 
through 
the 
PKJ, 
to 
use 
the 
credit 
proceeds. 
There 
will 
be 
one 
single 
SA 
for 
the 
whole 
project 
implementation. 
Project 
Agreements 
will 
be 
signed 
with 
each 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
involved 
in 
the project. 
The PKJ 
will 
execute 
the payments 
on 
behalf 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals, 
and 
of 
behalf 
of 
the 
villages 
selected 
acting 
as 
a 
payment 
'agent'. 
The 
Apa 
Canals 
will 
make their 
own 
payments 
in 
respect 
of 
their 
contributions 
to 
the 
project 
from 
their 
current 
accounts 
and 
may 
also 
contribute 
in 
kind 
(labor) 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project. 
In 
respect 
of 
the 
village 
water  supply 
component, 
the 
PKJ 
will 
execute 
the 
payments 
directly 
to 
the 
suppliers. 
This 
component 
involves 
water 
supply 
activities 
for 
the 
villages 
selected 
and 
funding 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
provided 
by 
grant 
financing 
that 
will 
be 
managed 
by 
the 
PIU 
in 
accordance 
with 
World 
Bank 
fiduciary 
procedures. 
The 
PKJ 
will 
receive 
these 
funds 
in 
a 
separate 
project 
special 
account 
and 
no 
funds 
will 
be 
transferred 
to 
beneficiaries 
(village 
communities) 
as 
there 
is 
very 
limited 
financial 
management 
capacity 
at the 
village 
level. 
D. 
Project Rationale 
1. 
Project 
alternatives 
considered 
and 
reasons 
for 
rejection: 
Pilot 
vs. 
full-scale 
project. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
be 
the 
Bank's 
first 
water 
sector 
investment 
in 
Moldova. 
Due 
to 
the current 
financial 
problems 
facing 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
and 
the 
serious 
contamination 
problems 
in 
the 
rural 
areas, 
a 
gradual 
and 
phased approach  was  selected 
as 
the 
best 
way to begin 
addressing 
the most urgent problems 
of 
the 
sector. 
Project 
Implementing 
Unit 
(PIU). 
The 
preference 
is 
to 
avoid 
establishing 
a 
separate 
project 
coordinatiodimplementation 
agency,  because 
it 
usually 
leads 
to 
limited 
capacity 
building 
in 
the various 
management, 
financial and 
technical 
areas, 
especially 
with 
stand-alone 
Utility 
projects. 
Due 
to 
the 
project's 
national 
coverage, 
however, 
it 
becomes 
necessary 
in 
this 
case 
to 
establish 
a 
project 
- 
15- 
Page  20
coordination/implementing 
unit. 
The 
unit 
is 
established 
within 
MECTD, which 
can 
benefit 
from 
the 
experience 
gained 
from 
the 
project. 
No 
Master 
Plan 
approach. 
The 
traditional 
approach 
of 
preparing a long-term investment 
plan 
based 
on 
a 
Master 
Plan  and 
detailed 
feasibility 
work 
was 
rejected 
because 
these 
conventional  supply-driven 
investment 
projects 
are 
not 
conducive 
to 
an 
efficient 
use 
of 
resources, 
both 
in 
cost 
and 
size. 
In 
lieu 
of 
that, 
a 
first-phase 
investment 
program 
was 
prepared 
during 
project 
preparation 
but 
remaining 
investment 
plans 
would 
be developed 
during 
the 
first 
year 
of 
project implementation 
building 
on 
the first-phase 
investment 
program 
and analysis. 
Local 
Operators. 
The 
options 
for 
local 
private 
sector 
participation 
considered were service contracts, 
management contracts and 
a simple 
form 
of 
contract operations 
where 
local 
operators 
would 
accept 
risks 
of 
collections 
and 
operations. 
The 
latter 
two 
have been discarded 
because 
contract 
operations 
with 
collections 
and 
operations 
risk 
represent 
a 
level 
of 
risk 
so 
high 
that 
bids 
would 
be 
unlikely. 
Management 
contracts 
would 
be 
expensive, 
without 
any 
real 
risk 
shifted 
to 
the 
local 
operator 
and therefore 
without 
any 
realistic promise 
of 
reduced 
costs. 
As 
a 
consequence, 
service  contracts 
are 
the 
only 
remaining 
realistic 
option 
for 
these 
selected 
Apa 
Canals. 
The 
precise 
scope 
of 
the 
service contracts 
was 
agreed 
upon 
with 
the 
participating 
Apa 
Canals. 
The 
service contracts 
would 
have an 
initial 
duration 
of 
2 
years, 
and 
their 
results 
would 
be 
evaluated 
6 
months 
before expiration 
so 
that a decision 
could 
be 
made 
on 
whether 
to 
extend 
them.

conditions, progress

PRGF

3. 
Lessons 
learned 
and 
reflected 
in 
the 
project 
design: 
The 
proposed 
Water 
Supply 
and 
Sanitation 
project 
would 
be 
the 
Bank's 
first 
operation 
in 
this 
sector 
in 
Moldova.  The 
lessons 
learned 
from 
overall 
Bank 
experience 
with 
projects 
and 
reflected 
in 
the 
proposed 
project 
design 
are 
notably 
the 
importance 
of: 
Learning 
lessons 
from 
the 
kinds 
of 
service contracts 
patterned 
on 
similar 
contracts 
used 
in 
Santiago 
de 
Chile 
and 
in 
Lima, 
Peru 
since  the 
early 
1980s 
and 
the 
mid-l990s, 
respectively. 
Those contracts have 
been 
successful 
in 
reducing 
costs 
by 
about 
20%. 
The 
sample contracts 
from 
these 
two 
cities 
have 
been 
used 
for 
the 
drafting 
of 
the 
contracts 
in 
the 
project 
cities 
in 
Moldova, 
although they 
have 
been 
adapted 
for 
the 
smaller 
size 
and 
for 
local 
conditions. 
One lesson gained 
from 
Santiago 
and 
Lima 
is 
the importance 
of 
having 
solid 
supervision 
of 
the 
quality 
of 
the 
service contractors' 
work; 
Creating client 
ownership at 
all 
levels 
of 
Government 
by 
preparing 
the 
project 
in 
close 
collaboration 
and 
implementation 
with 
the 
local 
stakeholders. 
Total 
commitment 
by 
the 
Ministry, 
Apa 
Canals and 
Municipal 
Governments 
is 
necessary 
to 
ensure 
that preparation 
and 
implementation 
proceed smoothly; 
Setting 
realistic 
and prudent 
efficiency 
improvement 
targets, 
including 
as 
a 
basis 
for 
sound 
financial 
projections. 
During 
project 
preparation, 
a 
Financial 
Action 
Plan 
has 
been 
agreed  upon, 
with 
a  specific 
collection 
increase 
program 
that 
will 
be 
updated 
and 
monitored 
biannually; 
Establishing 
a 
sound 
institutional framework 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
project 
is 
sustainable 
in 
this 
regard. 
A 
clear 
definition 
of 
project 
responsibilities 
and 
processes 
is 
necessary, 
which 
would 
be 
set 
forth 
in 
the 
project implementation 
plan; 
and 
Ensuring 
implementation 
readiness 
to  avoid  potential 
delays 
in 
the 
project 
after 
effectiveness. 
Bid 
documents 
are 
currently 
being 
prepared 
so 
that 
the 
first-year 
investments 
can 
be 
executed 
immediately 
after 
effectiveness. 
4. 
Indications 
of 
borrower 
commitment and 
ownership: 
The 
reforms 
associated 
with 
the 
proposed 
project 
design 
are 
significant 
and 
require 
exceptional 
borrower 
commitment 
and ownership. 
The 
Government 
has 
completed 
the 
National 
Water 
Action 
Plan 
to develop 
a 
sector 
reform 
program 
and strategy 
and 
to 
develop a 
financing 
plan 
for 
the sector. 
As 
part 
of 
the 
Action 
Plan, 
the 
Government 
has 
asked 
the 
Bank 
to 
assist 
with 
implementing 
sector 
refoms 
and 
to 
invest 
in 
the 
small and medium-sized 
cities 
most 
in 
need 
of 
water 
services and 
in 
those 
areas 
where 
other 
Donors 
are 
not working. 
Officials 
from 
MECTD, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and

Agriculture 
have 
all 
stated that 
the

urgent

Moldova 
is 
to 
enhance 
the

of

living 
in 
rural 
areas 
and 
medium-sized towns 
by 
improving 
access 
to 
safer 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
services. 
Each 
of 
the 
Ministries 
has 
been 
pressing 
the 
Bank for 
assistance 
to 
address 
these 
problems 
for 
the 
past 
5 
years. 
The 
Government 
has  also  asked  the 
Bank 
to 
establish  a 
Donor 
Coordination 
Task 
Force 
and take 
the 
lead 
in 
Donor 
coordination.

Cost 
Effectiveness 
of 
Rural 
Water Supply  Component. 
The 
second 
component 
to 
protect 
rural 
wells 
represents 
15% 
of 
total 
project 
costs. 
Rigorous 
economic 
cost-benefit 
analysis 
is 
an 
unsatisfactory 
methodology 
because 
the 
expected 
health 
benefits 
are 
exceedingly 
difficult to 
quantify. 
This 
component 
is 
still 
justified 
economically, 
however, 
because 
protection 
of 
rural 
shallow 
wells 
and 
sanitary 
education 
is 
arguably  the 
most 
cost-effective 
measure 
to 
safeguard 
the 
rural 
population 
from 
water  and 
sanitation-related 
disease. 
The 
proposed 
investment 
of 
US$1.5 
million 
amounts 
to 
US$20 
per 
rural 
dweller 
expected 
to 
benefit 
from 
expenditure  under 
this 
component. 
The 
proposed 
per 
capita 
expenditure 
could 
be 
compared 
with 
the 
cost 
of 
the 
new 
Danish-financed 
rural 
water 
supply 
investments 
with 
per 
capita 
of 
US$l20 
with 
the 
cost 
of 
the 
improvements 
of 
US$13 
per 
capita 
in 
the 
15 
small 
systems 
financed 
by 
the 
Social 
Infrastructure 
Fund 
in 
Moldova 
and 
with 
the 
range 
of 
rural 
water 
supply 
investments 
per 
capita 
of 
USSlOO 
in 
most developing 
countries. 
The activities 
to 
improve 
the 
health 
of 
the 
rural 
population who 
rely on 
simple 
shallow 
wells 
are 
also 
justified 
because 
of 
the

of

this

that comprises

the 
lowest 
income 
in 
Moldova.

Technical: 
The 
project 
would 
not

would 
focus 
instead 
on 
reduction 
of

through

During

that overall,  waste 
and

of 
water 
are 
enormous 
resulting

The 
Apa 
Canals' 
water

is

and  displays  enormous 
inefficiencies

urgent rehabilitation; 
Mechanical 
and

Pumps

The

The

are

Preventive

Only

The 
technical design 
of 
the 
project 
would 
target 
the 
huge

and

of

This

bulk;

Village 
Water Component 
The 
village 
component 
would 
finance urgent 
rehabilitation 
of 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
systems. 
This 
component 
would 
be 
contracted 
competitively 
to 
a 
local 
NGO 
or 
a 
consortium 
of 
NGOs 
to 
manage 
and 
supervise 
the 
day-to-day 
activities. 
A 
local 
firm 
would 
propose 
system improvements based 
on 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
Social 
Assessment. 
The 
terms 
of 
reference 
require 
the 
following: 
(i) 
an 
evaluation 
of 
the 
capability 
of 
any 
existing facilities; 
(ii) 
recommendations 
on 
which 
facilities 
should 
be 
replaced  and 
which 
should  be 
rehabilitated; 
(iii) 
recommendations 
of 
new 
facilities 
for 
expanding 
service 
to 
the 
poorest 
and unserved 
segments 
of 
the 
population 
in 
the three 
project 
communities; 
(iv) 
preparation 
of 
a procurement and 
works 
schedule 
for 
the 
recommended 
additions and 
improvements; 
and 
(v) 
estimates 
of 
costs. 
The 
procurement and 
works 
schedule 
would 
identify 
works 
that 
could 
be  carried 
out 
by 
the 
local 
population 
as 
an 
in-kind 
contribution. 
Based 
on 
the 
technical  report, 
MECTD, 
implementing 
NGO 
and 
the 
Bank 
would 
agree 
on 
the 
final 
project 
investment 
program 
for 
the 
communities and 
direct 
the 
local 
engineering 
firm 
to 
prepare 
final 
designs, 
technical specifications and 
bidding 
documents 
for 
the 
procurement 
of 
materials and 
works 
that 
are 
beyond 
the 
capacity 
of 
the 
local 
communities 
to 
carry 
out 
themselves. 
A 
local 
construction 
firm 
would 
be 
contracted 
to 
implement 
the 
works. 
The 
contract 
would 
include 
supervision 
of 
the 
in-kind 
contribution 
of 
the 
communities and 
carrying 
out 
works beyond 
the 
capacity 
of 
the 
beneficiaries. 
Large 
items 
of 
equipment 
and materials 
would 
be 
purchased 
under 
separate 
contracts 
by 
the 
implementing 
agency. 
Smaller  items 
would 
be 
provided 
by 
the 
works 
contractor. 
The 
local 
engineering 
firm 
who 
prepared 
the 
designs 
would 
monitor 
the 
work 
to 
ensure 
it 
was 
carried 
out 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
designs 
and 
specifications. 
The 
cost 
of 
this 
component 
includes 
the 
in-kind 
contribution. 
4. 
Institutional: 
The lack 
of 
institutional 
capacity 
would 
need 
to 
be 
enhanced 
in 
some 
of 
the 
weaker 
Apa 
Canals. 
The 
limited 
institutional 
capacity 
has 
been 
addressed 
through 
the 
simplicity 
of 
project 
design 
as 
well 
as 
appropriate 
training 
for 
both 
Apa 
Canals and 
PIU. 
Technical 
management 
and 
specialists 
are 
however, 
very 
capable 
in 
operating 
and 
maintaining 
the 
systems 
under stringent 
financial 
conditions 
and 
constant 
emergencies. 
With 
sufficient 
exposure 
to 
commercial 
billing 
and 
collection,  financial 
planning, 
accounting, 
network 
operations 
optimization 
and  water 
demand management programs, 
institutional 
capacity 
would 
be 
further 
strengthened 
during 
project 
implementation. 
4.1 
Executing 
agencies: 
The 
project 
would 
be 
implemented 
during 
2003-2007 
under 
the 
overall 
responsibility 
of 
the 
MECTD. 
The 
Ministry 
has 
already  established 
within 
its 
organization  a 
PIU. 
The 
PIU 
is 
working 
in 
close 
coordination 
with 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
and 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
all 
procurement, 
financial 
management 
and 
disbursement 
aspects. 
There 
would 
be 
one 
Special 
Account 
and 
all 
payments 
would 
be 
made 
directly 
from 
that account. 
- 
23 
- 
Page  28
4.2 
Project 
management: 
The 
project 
would 
be 
managed 
by 
the 
PIU 
in 
close 
collaboration 
with 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
and under the 
umbrella 
of 
MECTD 
and 
where 
needed, 
assisted 
by 
Technical 
Advisors. 
With 
this

MECTD 
and 
Apa 
Canals 
would 
have 
the  capacity 
to 
make investment 
decisions  and 
implement  the 
project. 
The  Technical  Advisors 
would 
assist 
MECTD 
and 
Apa 
Canals 
with 
identification 
and 
implementation 
of 
investments 
and improvements 
in 
network 
rehabilitation 
and 
operations, 
commercial 
practices and 
institutional 
strengthening, 
as 
described 
in 
the 
implementation 
section. 
4.3 
Procurement 
issues: 
The 
PIU 
would 
have 
primary 
responsibility 
for 
procurement. 
A 
procurement 
officer 
has 
been 
appointed 
by 
MECTD 
who 
would 
be trained 
under 
the 
project. 
While 
the 
Technical Advisors 
would 
be 
familiar 
with 
Bank 
procurement 
guidelines 
and 
coordinate 
preparation 
of 
procurement 
packages, 
consultants 
could 
be 
hired 
for 
the 
preparation 
of 
final 
designs 
and 
bidding 
documents, 
procurement 
assistance 
and 
implementation 
supervision 
for 
all

The Technical Advisors 
would 
coordinate 
this 
work, 
but 
would 
be 
responsible 
largely 
for 
identification 
and 
preparation 
of 
specifications 
for 
the investments. 
The 
following 
action 
plan 
would 
be 
implemented 
to 
strengthen 
the procurement capacity 
of 
the 
MECTD 
as 
well 
as 
to 
provide

and

to

for

a. 
The

was established 
18 
months 
ago 
and a Procurement 
Officer 
satisfactory 
to 
the 
Procurement 
Accredited 
Specialist (PAS) was 
hired 
in 
October 
2002. 
b. 
MECTD 
would

and

a

for

and assistant

PIU

This training 
would

completed 
within 
6 months 
of 
Credit

The

as

to produce

and 
the

to

for

4.4 
Financial 
management 
issues: 
The existing financial 
management arrangements 
of 
the 
project 
meet 
the 
minimum 
financial 
management 
requirements 
of 
the 
World 
Bank. 
As 
of 
the 
date 
of 
this 
report, 
the 
Borrower 
is 
in 
compliance 
with 
its 
audit 
covenants 
of 
existing 
Bank-financed projects. 
The 
annual audited 
project 
and 
Apa 
Canal 
entities 
financial 
statements 
will 
be 
provided to 
the 
Bank 
within 
six 
months 
of 
the 
end 
of 
each 
fiscal 
year and also 
at 
the 
closing 
of 
the 
project. 
The  current 
Country 
Financial  Accountability 
Assessment 
(CFAA) 
for 
Moldova 
as 
of 
September 
26, 
1995 was 
prepared 
at 
a 
time 
of 
considerable 
transition 
and therefore, 
in 
view 
of 
the 
time 
that 
has 
since 
elapsed, 
is 
considered 
to 
be 
out-of-date. 
A 
revised 
CFAA 
is 
under 
preparation. 
- 
24 
- 
Page  29
The 
I-PRSP 
of 
November 
15, 
2000 
confirms 
that 
improvement 
is 
required 
in 
the 
management 
of 
public 
expenditures, 
including 
the 
budget 
process 
and 
budget 
execution, 
as 
well 
as 
cash 
and 
debt 
management. 
Thus 
the 
PIU 
has developed 
policies 
and procedures 
that 
operate 
in 
addition 
to 
those 
of 
the 
current 
public 
expenditure 
management 
framework 
to minimize 
project financial 
management 
risks. 
In 
October 
2002, 
Bank 
conducted  a 
Country 
Portfolio 
Financial 
Management 
Review 
(CPFMR) 
of 
all 
projects 
under 
implementation 
in 
Moldova 
and 
identified 
some 
common 
financial 
management 
issues; 
the 
Bank 
confirmed 
that 
all 
such 
issues 
have been 
appropriately 
addressed 
in 
the 
design 
of 
project’s 
financial 
management arrangements. 
5. 
Environmental: 
5.1 
Summarize 
the 
steps 
undertaken 
for 
environmental 
assessment 
and 
EMP 
preparation 
(including 
consultation and 
disclosure) and the 
significant 
issues 
and 
their 
treatment 
emerging 
from 
this 
analysis. 
The project 
would 
have 
no 
negative 
environmental  effects 
and 
would 
not 
raise any 
resettlement 
or 
property 
rights 
issues,  because 
it 
focuses 
on 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
existing 
network 
and 
facilities. 
The 
project 
would 
cover 
only 
rehabilitation, 
not 
extension 
of 
services. 
All 
work would 
be 
executed 
either 
within 
existing 
facilities or 
within 
rights 
of 
way 
of 
public 
street 
networks. 
Bidding 
documents 
would 
specify requirements 
for 
keeping 
work 
sites 
pollution-free, 
returning 
sites 
to 
their 
original 
conditions 
and 
minimizing 
dust, 
noise 
and 
related 
work 
nuisance. 
Environmental 
Category: 
B 
(Partial 
Assessment) 
The 
project 
is 
expected 
to 
bring 
environmental 
and health 
benefits 
because: 
(i) 
rehabilitation 
and 
improved 
operation 
and maintenance 
of 
the 
water 
network 
would 
reduce water losses 
and 
decrease 
the 
volume 
of 
water  intake 
and 
sewage; 
and 
(ii) 
improvement 
in 
operations 
would 
contribute 
to 
better 
day-to-day 
environmental 
management 
(reduction 
of 
leakage, 
lowering 
operating 
costs 
related 
to 
energy 
use, 
better  sanitation practices 
and maintenance 
of 
the 
sewerage 
network 
and better compliance 
with 
environmental regulations 
and 
Moldavian 
international 
obligations). 
The long-term 
impacts 
of 
the 
project 
are 
clearly 
positive 
both 
in 
terms 
of 
local 
and global 
benefits. 
The 
investments 
financed 
by 
the 
project 
would 
not 
affect 
any 
known 
archeological 
or historical 
site 
or 
any 
natural 
habitat, 
nor 
would 
they 
affect 
indigenous 
people. 
No 
dams 
are 
in 
the 
project 
scope. 
No 
resettlement 
is 
envisaged 
in 
the 
project 
scope. 
For 
the 
village 
component, 
up 
to 
10 
villages 
will 
be 
included 
in 
the 
project 
based 
on 
the 
same 
criteria 
for 
the 
urban 
cities.  Water networks and intakes 
are 
the 
property 
of 
the 
local 
authorities 
in 
these 
towns. 
Water 
from 
artesian 
wells 
without 
any 
treatment 
is 
being 
pumped 
into 
the 
distribution 
network. 
Many 
houses 
have 
self-dug 
wells. 
Preliminary 
studies 
conducted 
by 
the 
National 
Sanitary 
Inspection 
and 
confirmed 
by 
the 
Social 
Assessment 
have 
shown 
the 
high 
pollution 
of 
both 
artesian 
and 
self-dug 
wells 
by 
agncultural 
and domestic 
waste 
as 
well 
as 
poorly 
managed storage 
of 
agrochemicals. 
The villages do 
not 
have 
centralized 
wastewater 
systems. 
Wastewater 
is 
collected 
into 
latrines 
and 
cesspools. 
There 
is 
limited 
monitoring 
of 
water 
quality 
in 
wells 
and 
the 
local 
population 
is 
rarely 
informed 
about 
the 
water 
quality. 
The 
project 
will 
provide technical 
assistance 
for 
water 
well 
protection 
and 
conduct a 
national 
hygiene 
promotion 
program 
for rural 
communities. 
International 
Waterways 
Exemption 
to 
OP/BP 7.50 
It 
has 
been 
assessed 
that 
the 
project falls 
within 
the exceptions 
to 
the 
notification 
requirement 
under 
paragraph 
7(a) 
of 
the 
Policy. 
According to 
paragraph 7 
of 
OP 
7.50, 
there 
are 
exceptions 
to 
the Bank’s 
requirement 
that the other 
riparian

be 
notified 
of 
the 
project. 
According to 
paragraph 
7(a) 
of 
OP 
7.50, 
the 
exception 
applies: 
“For  any 
ongoing 
schemes, 
projects 
involving 
additions 
or 
alterations that 
require 
rehabilitation, 
construction, 
or 
other 
changes 
that 
in 
the 
judgment 
of 
the 
Bank 
(i) 
would 
not 
- 
25 
- 
Page  30
adversely 
change 
the 
quality or 
quantity 
of 
water 
flows to 
the other 
riparians; 
and 
(ii) 
would 
not 
be 
adversely 
affected 
by 
the other 
riparians‘ possible water 
use. 
This 
exception 
applies 
only 
to 
minor 
additions 
or 
alterations 
to 
the 
ongoing 
scheme; 
it 
does 
not 
cover 
works 
and 
activities 
that 
would 
exceed 
the 
original 
scheme, 
change 
its 
nature, 
or 
so 
alter 
or 
expand 
its 
scope 
and extent 
as 
to 
make 
it 
appear 
a 
new 
or 
different 
scheme. 
In 
case 
of 
doubt 
regarding 
the 
extent 
to 
which 
a 
project 
meets the 
criteria 
of 
this 
exception, 
the 
executive 
directors representing 
the 
riparians 
concerned 
are 
informed 
and 
given 
at 
least 
two 
months to 
reply. 
Even 
if 
projects 
meet 
the 
criteria 
of 
this 
exception, the 
Bank 
tries 
to 
secure 
compliance 
with 
the 
requirements 
of 
any 
agreement 
or 
arrangement 
between 
the riparians.” 
The 
project 
team 
has 
assessed 
the 
project 
components 
in 
the 
context 
of 
and 
relation 
to 
OP 
7.50. 
The 
OP 
7.50 
applies 
to 
the 
project 
as 
the 
cities 
of 
Cahul, 
Orhei 
and 
Soroca 
are 
located 
on 
international 
waterways 
as 
defined

l(a)
