The 
City 
of 
Cahul 
in 
Southern 
Moldova 
is 
located 
about 
10 
km 
from 
the 
Romanian border 
on 
the 
Prut 
River. 
The 
Prut  originates 
in 
Ivano-Frankivsk, Oblast 
of 
Ukraine 
and 
flows along 
the 
Romania-Ukraine 
border 
until 
it 
inflows into 
the 
Danube 
River, 
whose watershed 
is 
shared 
by 
more 
than 
20 
states 
in 
Europe. 
The 
Danube 
is 
the 
primary 
watercourse 
flowing 
into 
the 
Black 
Sea. 
Cahul 
is 
the 
6th 
largest 
town 
in 
Moldova 
with 
a 
population 
of 
about 
44,000. 
There 
are 
about  25 
enterprises 
that 
are 
the 
major 
employers and about 78 
separate 
budget entitieslconsumers. 
The 
City 
of 
Orhei 
is 
located 
on 
the 
Rht 
River 
(originates 
in 
Moldova), 
a 
tributary 
of 
the 
Nistru 
River 
about 80 
km 
from 
its 
inflow 
into 
the 
Nistru 
River. 
The 
Nistru 
River 
originates 
in 
Lviv, 
Oblast 
of 
Ukraine, 
flows 
through 
Moldova 
and 
then through 
Odessa, 
Oblast 
of 
Ukraine, 
draining 
into 
the 
Black 
Sea. 
Orhei 
uses 
underground water 
as 
the 
source 
for 
intake and 
discharges processed wastewater 
into 
the 
RZut 
River. 
Orhei 
is 
the 
10th 
largest 
town 
in 
Moldova 
with 
a 
population 
of 
about 
37,000. 
There 
are 
about 
23 
enterprises 
in 
the 
city, 
and about 
55 
budget consumers 
(e.g.schools, 
libraries, hospitals and 
administrative 
buildings). 
The 
City 
of 
Soroca 
is 
located along 
the 
Nistru 
River 
which 
is 
the 
water 
intake 
source 
for 
the 
city. 
Soroca 
is 
an 
important 
port 
in 
Northern 
Moldova 
located 
on 
the 
border 
with 
Ukraine 
on 
the 
Nistru 
River, 
about 
400 
km 
from 
its 
inflow 
into 
the 
Black 
Sea. 
It 
is 
the 
7th 
largest 
town 
with 
a 
population 
of 
about 
40,000 
people. 
It 
has 
about 8 
large 
employers 
and 
47 
budget 
entities. 
The 
main 
source 
of 
income 
to 
the 
city 
is 
remittances 
from 
workers 
in 
Russia, 
Romania 
and 
other countries. 
The 
project 
team 
review 
of 
known 
international 
arrangements 
that 
exist between 
Moldova 
and 
other 
riparians 
of 
the 
international 
waterways 
involved 
in 
this 
project 
(i.e., 
Black 
Sea, 
Nistru 
River, 
Prut 
kver 
and 
the 
Danube 
River) 
has 
identified 
three 
multi-Governmental 
agreements 
of 
relevance: 
(i) 
Convention 
for 
the 
Protection 
of 
the 
Black 
Sea 
against 
Pollution 
(Bucharest 
Convention, 
1992); 
(ii) 
Odessa 
Ministerial 
Declaration 
on 
the 
Protection 
of 
the 
Black 
Sea 
Environment 
(Odessa 
Declaration, 
1993); 
and 
(iii) 
Convention 
on 
Co-operation 
for 
the 
Protection 
and 
Sustainable 
Use 
of 
the 
River 
Danube 
(short 
title: 
Danube 
River 
Protection 
Convention-DRPC,  Sofia, 
1994). 
The 
Bucharest 
Convention includes 
a basic 
framework 
of 
agreement 
and three 
specific protocols, 
which 
are: 
(i) 
the 
control 
of 
land-based 
sources 
of 
pollution; 
(ii) 
dumping 
of 
waste; 
and 
(iii) 
joint 
action 
in 
the 
case 
of 
accidents (such 
as 
oil 
spills). 
The 
Odessa 
Declaration 
is 
largely 
based 
upon 
the 
Agenda 
2 
1 
adopted 
at 
the 
UN 
Conference 
on 
Environment 
and 
Development 
in 
1992. 
The 
Danube 
River 
Protection Convention  calls  for: 
(i) 
conservation, 
improvement 
and 
the 
rational 
use 
of 
surface 
and 
ground 
waters 
in 
the catchment 
area; 
and 
(ii) 
control 
of 
the 
hazards 
originating 
from 
accidents 
involving 
substances 
hazardous 
to 
water, 
floods 
and ice-hazards, 
-26- 
Page  31
and contributes 
to 
reducing 
the 
pollution 
loads 
of 
the 
Black 
Sea 
from 
sources 
in 
the 
catchment 
area. 
Several 
bilateral 
agreements 
between 
Moldova 
and 
its 
neighbors 
on 
the 
mutual 
use 
of 
water resources are 
currently 
under 
development. 
Activities 
financed 
by 
the 
project 
would 
support 
the objectives 
of 
the 
ongoing 
Black 
Sea 
Environmental 
Program 
(World 
BanMUNDPIGEF), 
Lower 
Prut 
Conservation 
Project 
(GEF) 
and 
DABLAS 
(Danube-Black 
Sea 
Task 
Force) 
of 
the 
European 
Commission 
activities. 
The 
designs 
and 
tender 
documents 
to 
be 
prepared 
under 
the 
project 
would aim 
at 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
ongoing 
schemes 
and 
construction 
of 
wastewater objects, 
which 
in 
the 
team's 
judgment 
are: 
(i) 
minor 
alterations 
to 
the 
ongoing 
scheme; 
(ii) 
would 
not 
adversely 
change 
the 
quality 
and 
reduce 
quantity through 
leakage management 
of 
water 
flows 
to 
other 
riparians; and 
(iii) 
would not 
be 
adversely 
affected 
by 
the 
other 
riparians' water 
use. 
Considering 
the nature 
of 
the 
project, 
the 
exception 
to 
the 
external 
notification 
requirements 
of 
OP 
7.50, 
set 
forth 
in 
paragraph 
7(a) 
of 
OP 
7.50, 
is 
fblly 
applicable. 
5.2 
What 
are 
the 
main 
features 
of 
the 
EMP 
and 
are 
they 
adequate? 
EMP 
is 
required 
for 
the 
project 
to 
ensure 
that the 
rehabilitation 
and operation 
of 
the 
water 
supply 
systems 
would 
meet  the 
requirements 
of 
the 
World 
Bank 
environmental 
assessment 
policy 
as 
described 
in 
OPIBPIGP 
4.01, 
as 
well 
as 
Moldavian 
laws and 
regulations 
on 
environmental 
expertise  and 
impact 
assessment, 
and 
would follow 
the 
best 
environmental 
practices. 
Separate 
EA 
for 
the 
project 
is 
not 
required, 
considering 
that 
only 
rehabilitation works 
are 
envisaged. 
The 
EMP 
covers 
the 
following: 
(i) 
management 
of 
water 
resources 
including 
the 
abstraction 
of 
groundwater 
from 
surface 
and  underground  water 
sources, 
drinking 
water  quality; 
and 
(ii) 
water 
distribution 
operations  and 
prevention 
of 
water 
wastage 
from 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
operations 
and 
by 
consumers. 
MECTD will 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
implementing 
and 
monitoring 
the 
EMP. 
MECTD 
controls 
the 
standards 
for 
drinlung 
water 
and wastewater 
effluent 
quality. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and 
its 
State 
Sanitary 
Inspection 
controls 
the 
standards 
for 
potable water 
safety 
and 
the 
effluent quality. 
The 
project 
was 
classified 
as 
a 
Category 
B, 
as 
it 
is 
not 
likely 
to 
have 
significant 
adverse 
environmental 
impacts that 
are 
sensitive, 
diverse 
or 
unprecedented. 
The 
potential 
adverse 
environmental  impacts 
on 
human 
population 
or 
environmentally important 
areas, 
including 
wetlands, forests, 
grasslands 
and other 
natural 
habitats, 
are 
site-specific;  few, 
if 
any 
of 
them 
are 
irreversible. 
No 
such 
impacts 
are 
associated 
with 
the 
project. 
5.3 
For 
Category 
A 
and 
B 
projects, 
timeline 
and 
status 
of 
EA: 
Date 
of 
receipt 
of 
final 
draft: 
August 
2002 
5.4 
How 
have stakeholders 
been 
consulted 
at 
the 
stage 
of 
(a) 
environmental 
screening and 
(b) 
draft 
EA 
report 
on 
the 
environmental impacts 
and 
proposed environment 
management 
plan? 
Describe 
mechanisms 
of 
consultation 
that were used and 
which 
groups were consulted? 
- 
27 
- 
Page  32
The 
main 
objectives 
of 
the 
public 
consultations 
are 
to: 
(i) 
provide information to 
users 
and 
other 
key 
stakeholders 
about 
the 
improved 
water 
systems, 
the 
risks 
associated 
with 
poorly 
managed  water 
networks, 
the 
costs 
of 
improvements, and 
their 
management 
responsibilities; and 
(ii) 
involve 
the 
public 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
project 
support. 
A 
“stakeholder” 
is 
any 
person, group, 
or 
institution 
that 
has 
an 
interest 
in 
a water 
supply 
and sanitation 
plan or 
activity. 
Stakeholders 
include 
those that 
are 
directly 
affected, 
either 
positively 
or 
negatively, 
by 
an 
action 
or policy; 
those 
who 
have 
direct 
management 
responsibilities; 
and 
those 
who 
may 
have  special 
knowledge 
or 
skills 
that  can 
be 
applied 
to 
WWS 
planning, 
decision 
making, 
or 
operations. 
The 
following 
persons 
and 
groups have 
key 
roles: 
residential 
consumers, 
commercial 
consumers, 
institutional 
and 
industrial 
user, 
representatives 
from 
the 
ethnic 
communities, 
local 
administration, 
utility 
workers, 
and 
public 
health 
and environment 
inspectors. 
The 
meetings 
took 
place 
in 
Cahul, 
Orhei 
and  Soroca 
during 
November 
2002. 
The 
water 
utility 
was 
responsible 
for 
inviting 
stakeholders. 
All 
utilities 
prepared 
a 
protocol 
of 
the 
meeting 
and 
submitted 
to 
the 
PIT 
and 
the 
PTU 
for 
the 
final 
approval. 
The 
EMP 
was 
disclosed 
in 
Cahul 
on 
November 
14, 
2002; 
Orhei 
on 
November 
14,2002; 
and 
Soroca 
on 
November 
17,2002. 
The 
EMP 
was 
sent 
to 
the 
Infoshop 
on 
November 
14,2002. 
The 
meeting 
discussed 
the 
following: 
Topic 
1: 
Public 
Information 
and 
Awareness 
Raising 
This 
task 
involves 
providing 
general consumer 
information 
to 
the 
public 
about 
the 
new 
system; 
the 
risks 
associated 
with 
inadequate 
WWS; 
the 
role 
of 
individuals, 
governments, 
and 
other  institutions, 
and 
nongovernmental 
organizations 
in 
the 
WWS; 
and 
basic consumer 
information. 
The 
utility 
described 
a 
plan 
with 
the 
local 
authority 
on 
disseminating 
basic 
information 
about 
the 
improved 
WWS, 
including 
schedule 
of 
works, 
requirements 
for 
project 
development, 
etc., 
This 
kind 
of 
information 
should be 
published 
in 
a 
local 
newspaper, 
disseminated 
to 
all 
citizens, 
and 
be 
available 
at 
all 
official 
places 
(e.g., 
mayors 
office, 
water 
utility, 
and other 
places). 
The 
utility 
discussed 
at 
the 
meeting 
and later 
will 
carry  out a 
special 
publicity 
campaign 
designed 
to 
publicize 
the 
need 
to 
pay 
increased 
charges 
for 
WWS 
prior 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
actual 
service improvements. 
Topic 
2: 
Promote 
Public 
Participation 
in Project 
Implementation 
Public 
participation 
is 
an 
integral part 
of 
this 
planning 
process 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
plan 
takes 
into 
account 
the 
needs 
and 
concerns 
of 
the 
users 
the 
system 
is 
intended 
to 
serve. 
Public participation 
will 
be 
particularly 
important 
for 
the 
planning 
and 
development 
of 
any  large 
construction 
and 
rehabilitation 
works. 
While 
the adjacent 
residential 
population 
may 
understand 
the 
need 
for 
such works, 
they 
would 
rather 
have 
them located 
elsewhere. 
Overcoming 
this 
attitude 
requires general 
public 
understanding 
of 
the 
requirements 
of 
effective 
WWS, 
successful 
communication, 
and 
participation 
of 
the 
concerned 
community 
in 
siting 
decisions. 
Social 
The Social 
Assessment 
carried 
out 
by 
a 
local 
NGO 
is 
targeted 
to 
both 
municipal 
and 
rural 
communities. 
The 
structure 
of 
the 
analysis varies 
according 
to 
the 
two 
target populations. 
The 
rural 
Social 
Assessment 
consists 
of 
a 
sample 
of 
households 
in 
about 
40 
villages, focus 
groups 
in 
sample 
villages, 
and 
interviews 
with 
local 
administrative 
officials. 
The 
municipal 
Social 
Assessment also 
includes focus groups 
of 
users, 
water 
user 
associations, NGOs, 
and dialogue 
with 
the 
management 
of 
the 
pilot 
Apa 
Canals. 
It 
provides 
the 
basis 
for 
a 
strategy 
for 
developing 
an 
external 
relations program 
with 
users 
and other 
stakeholders. 
- 
28 
- 
Page  33
The 
combined Social 
Assessment 
provides 
a basis 
for 
reviewing 
the 
preliminary 
analysis 
provided 
by 
consultants assisting the 
Bank 
team and 
validating 
the 
design 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
questions asked 
in 
the 
social 
assessment 
relate 
to 
the 
following 
broad 
issues: 
(i) 
knowledge 
about 
water 
quality 
problems and 
their 
impact 
on 
health; 
(ii) 
opinion 
on 
the 
need 
to 
improve 
water 
supply; 
(iii) 
coping 
mechanisms, 
and 
(iv) 
the 
community's 
willingness 
to 
participate 
in 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
projects. 
The 
findings 
confirm 
that 
access 
to 
water  and 
sanitation 
are 
critical 
problems 
and 
the 
widespread 
dissatisfaction 
with 
current 
provision 
of 
water 
services. 
About 
half 
of 
the 
population 
surveyed 
in 
rural 
areas 
listed 
water and 
sanitation 
as 
among 
the 
two 
most 
acute 
problems 
in 
their 
community. 
Sixty-seven 
percent 
saw 
water 
as 
a  serious 
or 
very 
serious 
problem. 
About 
40% 
spent 
2 
to 
4 
hours 
each day 
gathering 
water. 
Sixty-two 
percent 
also 
saw 
a 
direct 
economic 
impact 
on 
their 
lives 
as 
a 
result 
of 
limited 
access 
to 
water. 
Users 
are 
quick 
to 
link 
health 
problems 
to 
the 
poor quality 
of 
available 
water, 
but, 
especially 
in 
rural 
areas, 
there 
is 
considerable 
variation 
from 
community 
to 
community 
in 
the 
application 
of 
this perception 
to 
everyday 
use. 
The 
most common 
sources 
of 
pollution 
cited included 
43% 
from 
garbage, 
25% 
from 
domestic  animals, 
and 
about 
15% 
as 
a 
result 
of 
seepage 
from 
toilets 
and 
household 
waste  water. 
Information 
programs 
could 
be 
a 
helpful 
measure 
in 
improving 
everyday 
practice 
in 
rural 
areas. 
Water 
distribution 
systems 
are 
in 
extremely 
poor condition 
at 
both 
the 
municipal 
and 
rural 
level. 
Users 
complain 
of 
lack 
of 
pressure, 
poor 
quality, 
and 
irregularity 
in 
services. 
Water 
that 
was 
uncontaminated 
at 
the 
source 
often 
is 
contaminated 
in 
route 
to 
the 
consumer, 
and water 
losses 
from 
leaks 
is 
considerable. 
About 
83% 
of 
those 
surveyed 
in 
rural 
areas 
depended 
on 
wells 
or 
springs 
for 
the 
water, and 
only 
about 
4% 
had 
access 
to 
piped 
water. 
Of 
those 
having 
access 
to piped 
water 
only 
12% 
had 
water 
continuously 
and 
about 
half 
stated 
that 
there was 
never any 
water. 
Of 
those 
lacking 
accessed 
to piped 
water, about 
60% 
used 
well 
water 
exclusively 
and 
only 
about 30% ever purchased 
bottled 
water. 
About 
54% 
felt 
the 
local 
mayor's 
office 
role 
in 
improving 
the 
supply 
of 
potable  water 
was 
unsatisfactory 
or 
very 
unsatisfactory. 
About 
50% 
felt 
that  responsibilities 
for 
investment 
in 
local 
water 
systems 
should 
be 
handled 
by 
water 
user 
associations 
or local 
village 
enterprises. 
The 
project 
seeks 
to 
balance 
impact 
on 
rural 
and 
municipal 
populations 
but 
is 
especially 
concerned 
with 
impact 
upon 
the 
poorest 
segments 
of 
society. 
At 
this 
level 
women and 
children 
are 
often 
burdened 
with 
water-gathering 
tasks 
and 
are 
also 
especially  vulnerable 
to 
health 
issues. 
The 
Social 
Assessment 
is 
intended to 
assist 
fine 
tuning 
the 
implementation 
process 
and 
targeting 
the 
most vulnerable 
population 
groups. 
Improved 
health 
for 
children 
in 
rural 
areas, 
for 
example, 
is 
a 
particular 
priority. 
Improved 
water 
systems 
for rural 
schools 
would 
be 
a 
prime 
target 
for 
investment. 
Capacity 
building 
for 
local 
institutions 
and 
self-help 
programs 
will 
be 
highlighted 
during 
implementation. 
A 
rural 
assessment 
was 
also undertaken. 
The 
assessment 
incorporates 
both 
standard 
household 
surveys 
and 
participatory 
rapid 
appraisal 
(PRA) 
methodology. 
This 
methodology 
enables 
enhanced 
access 
to 
user 
perspectives, 
both 
in 
terms 
of 
immediacy 
and 
lower 
costs. 
The 
application 
of 
the 
methodology 
to 
the appraisal 
of 
other 
issues 
and 
projects 
is 
expected 
to 
be an 
indirect 
benefit 
for 
the 
assessment 
process. 
Social 
development  outcomes 
will 
be 
monitored 
through 
the  continuous 
use 
of 
the 
instruments 
used 
during 
identification 
and 
appraisal. 
The 
results 
of 
the 
initial 
assessments 
will 
be 
reviewed 
at 
appraisal 
to 
determine 
key 
elements 
to 
be 
tracked 
during 
project  implementation 
to 
indicate 
outcomes 
and 
user 
satisfaction. 
These 
will 
be 
integrated 
into 
the 
performance 
indicator 
set 
provided 
in 
Annex 
6.5. 
The 
mix 
of 
instruments 
will 
be 
determined 
after 
the 
review 
of 
the 
social 
assessment 
during 
appraisal. 
During 
the 
- 
29 
- 
Page  34
first 
2 
years 
the 
monitoring 
process 
will 
take 
place 
biannually, 
and thereafter 
once 
annually. 
The 
monitoring 
will 
be 
conducted either 
prior 
to 
or 
in 
conjunction 
with 
supervision missions 
by 
local 
groups. 
A 
social 
specialist 
will 
participate 
in 
the 
supervision missions 
to 
assure 
quality control 
and consolidate 
the 
results 
of 
the 
monitoring. 
The 
monitoring 
process 
will 
build 
upon 
the 
lessons 
learned 
by 
the 
current 
Social 
Assessment 
in 
the 
conduct 
of 
its 
initial 
assessment. 
During 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project, 
the 
monitoring 
would 
be 
provided 
by 
a 
consortium 
of 
user groups 
and 
NGOs. 
A 
conceptual 
workshop 
would 
be 
held 
to 
play 
a 
coordinating 
role 
to 
ensure 
methodological 
consistency. 
The 
development 
of 
the 
Customer  Service 
Department 
for 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
would 
assume 
either 
the 
collaboration 
in 
or 
assumption 
of 
responsibility 
for 
such 
assessments 
on 
a 
long-term 
basis 
by 
the 
Apa 
Canals. 
5.5 
What 
mechanisms have 
been 
established 
to monitor 
and evaluate the 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
on 
the 
environment? 
Do 
the 
indicators 
reflect 
the 
objectives 
and 
results 
of 
the 
EMP? 
MECTD 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
EMP 
implementation 
and 
monitoring. 
The 
summary 
of 
the 
project’s environmental 
issues, 
mitigation 
program, 
and 
associated costs 
is 
summarized 
in 
Annexes 
A-F 
of 
the 
EMP. 
This 
includes 
monitoring 
costs, 
technical 
assistance 
associated 
with 
water 
resource 
management, 
environmental 
expertise  and 
technical 
assistance 
with 
the  development 
of 
legal 
and 
institutional 
framework. The 
budget 
for 
implementing 
the 
EMP 
in 
all 
project 
sites 
is 
US$200,000. 
The conditions 
of 
the 
rehabilitation 
works 
contract 
includes 
requirements 
for 
the 
work 
to 
be 
performed 
as 
per 
international 
specifications. 
The 
contracts 
would 
be 
properly 
prepared 
and 
supervised 
to minimize 
disturbance 
and 
air 
and water 
pollution. 
Monitoring 
of 
the 
impact 
during 
the 
construction 
phase 
would 
be 
undertaken 
by 
the Specialist 
in 
the 
PIU 
and the 
Municipal 
Department 
for 
Environment.  Dedicated 
and 
fully 
trained 
personnel 
would 
be 
appointed 
to 
carry 
out 
this 
monitoring. 
In 
Cahul, 
the 
governmental 
agencies 
Sanitary 
Epidemiological 
Station  and 
Environmental Agency 
provide 
continuous 
quality control 
of 
potable 
water  and 
water 
quality 
at 
intake. 
The Cahul 
Apa 
Canal 
performs 
its 
own 
testing 
of 
the 
water 
quality 
at 
intake 
and 
before 
the 
distribution 
network. 
Information 
on 
wastewater discharge 
is 
submitted 
to 
the 
National 
Statistical 
Department. 
In 
Orhei, 
the 
Apa 
Canal  and Academy 
of 
Sciences 
and 
Technical 
University 
of 
Moldova 
perform 
monitoring 
of 
the 
condition 
of 
water 
intakes 
and 
that 
of 
the 
wastewater treatment 
plant. The 
Regional 
Center 
of 
Preventive 
Medicine 
of 
Orhei 
establishes 
the 
schedule 
for 
tests 
of 
potable 
water 
quality. 
There 
are 
two 
control 
groups 
of 
potablewater.  Wastewater 
analyses are 
provided 
on 
a 
daily 
basis 
(the 
main 
parameters); 
and 
the 
complete 
wastewater 
analyses 
are 
performed 
once 
every 
10 
days 
by 
the 
analytical 
laboratory 
of 
Orhei 
Apa 
Canal. 
Information 
of 
the 
discharges 
is 
presented 
to 
the 
Territorial 
Environmental Agency 
on 
a 
quarterly 
basis. 
In 
Soroca, 
the 
governmental 
agencies 
Sanitary 
Epidemiological 
Station  and 
Environmental Agency 
provide 
continuous 
quality control 
of 
potable water  and water 
quality 
at 
intake.  The 
Apa 
Canal 
also 
performs 
its 
own 
tests 
of 
the 
water 
quality 
at 
intake and 
the 
distribution 
network. 
The 
wastewater 
is 
collected and then 
processed 
at 
the 
waste water treatment 
plant 
in 
Ukraine. 
- 
30 
- 
Page  35
Rural 
Water 
Suuply 
The 
village 
User Associations 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
routine 
and 
preventive 
operation 
and 
maintenance, 
routine 
management 
tasks 
and decision-making 
on 
water 
system development 
and 
expansion 
of 
coverage, 
health 
and 
behavior 
change 
motivation. 
It 
will 
protect  the 
water 
source, 
watershed, 
and 
manage 
sanitation and 
waste 
collection 
in 
environmentally 
sustainable manner. 
The 
NGO 
will 
provide 
continuous 
technical 
assistance 
for 
the 
project 
implementation, 
including 
technical advice 
and 
specialist services. 
It 
will 
help 
with 
technical  and  social 
aspects 
of 
the  system  development 
and 
expansion, 
advice 
on 
regulation, 
standards 
and 
legal 
issues,and 
also 
on 
fund-raising,  leverage 
of 
funds, 
procedures 
and 
grant 
applications. 
The 
PIU 
will 
monitor 
all 
activities related 
to 
this 
component 
and 
support tendering 
and 
disbursement. 
All 
of 
these 
issues 
are 
specified 
in 
the Operational 
Manual 
for 
the 
project. 
6. 
Social: 
6.1 
Summarize 
key 
social 
issues 
relevant 
to 
the 
project 
objectives, and 
specify the project's social 
development outcomes. 
The 
scarcity 
of 
potable 
water 
is 
a 
serious 
problem 
in 
Moldova, 
especially 
for 
the 
rural 
communities 
that 
make 
up 
more 
than 
half 
of 
the 
population 
of 
the 
country. 
Only 
17% 
of 
the 
rural 
communities 
have 
piped 
water 
supply 
systems, 
and 67% 
of 
these 
are 
in 
poor condition 
and 
provide 
water  that 
does 
not 
meet 
hygienic 
norms. 
The 
majority 
of 
the 
wells 
serving 
the 
rest 
of 
the 
rural population 
are 
polluted. 
The 
development 
objective 
of 
the 
project 
directly 
addresses 
this 
most 
serious 
constraint 
on 
the 
health 
and 
well 
being 
of 
the 
population. 
The 
specific social 
development outcomes 
for 
the 
project 
would 
include: 
(i) 
increased 
access 
to 
potable 
water, 
and 
(ii) 
the 
empowerment 
of 
water users 
through 
elected 
representatives 
in 
water 
user-groups 
in 
the management 
of 
the 
provision 
of 
water 
services. 
The 
Social 
Assessment 
targeted 
both 
municipal 
and 
rural 
communities. 
The 
structure 
of 
the 
analysis 
varied 
according 
to 
the 
two 
target 
populations. 
The 
municipal 
Social 
Assessment 
conducted household 
surveys 
in 
18 
municipalities 
and consulted 
with 
about 80 specialists 
in 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
issues. 
It 
also 
incorporated 
the 
conduct 
of 
focus groups 
in 
five 
different 
municipalities; 
the 
participants 
included 
representatives 
from 
the 
Apa 
Canals, 
the 
offices 
of 
the 
mayors, 
environmental 
specialists, 
health 
and 
medical 
professionals, 
NGOs 
and 
joumalists. 
There 
was 
also 
a media campaign 
linked 
to 
the 
public 
review 
of 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
issues, 
which 
provides 
the 
basis 
for 
a 
strategy 
for 
developing 
an 
extemal 
relations 
program 
with 
users 
and other 
stakeholders. 
The 
rural 
Social 
Assessment 
consisted 
of 
household 
surveys 
in 
23 
communities, 
focus 
groups 
in 
35 
villages  and 
interviews 
with 
local 
administrative 
officials 
and 
specialists 
in 
19 
communities. 
The 
combined Social 
Assessment 
has 
constituted the 
basis 
for 
reviewing 
the 
preliminary 
analysis 
provided 
by 
consultants assisting the 
Bank 
team 
and 
has 
validated 
the 
design 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
questions asked 
in 
the 
Social 
Assessment 
related 
to 
the 
following 
broad 
issues: 
(i) 
knowledge 
about 
water 
quality 
problems 
and 
their 
impact 
on 
health; 
(ii) 
opinion 
on 
the 
need 
to 
improve 
water 
supply; 
(iii) 
coping 
mechanisms; and 
(iv) 
the 
community's willingness 
to 
participate 
in 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
projects. 
The 
findings 
confirm 
that 
access 
to 
water and sanitation 
is 
a 
critical 
problem 
and that dissatisfaction 
with 
current 
provision 
of 
water services 
is 
widespread. 
About 
half 
of 
the 
population 
surveyed 
in 
rural 
areas 
listed 
water and 
sanitation 
as 
among 
the 
two 
most 
acute 
problems 
in 
their 
community; 
67% 
saw 
water 
as 
a 
serious 
or 
very 
serious 
problem; 
about 
40% 
spent 
2 
to 
4 
hours 
each day 
gathering water 
and 
62% 
also 
saw 
a direct 
economic 
impact 
on 
their 
lives 
as 
a 
result 
of 
limited 
access 
to water. 
-31 
- 
Page  36
Users 
were 
quick to 
link 
health 
problems 
to 
the 
poor 
quality 
of 
available 
water, 
but 
especially 
in 
rural 
areas, 
there 
is 
considerable 
variation 
from 
community 
to 
community 
in 
the 
application 
of 
this 
perception 
to 
everyday 
use. 
The 
most 
common 
sources 
of 
pollution 
cited included 
43% 
from 
garbage, 
25% 
from 
domestic  animals 
and 
about 
15% 
as 
a 
result 
of 
seepage 
from 
toilets  and 
household 
wastewater. 
Information 
programs 
under 
the 
project 
would 
be 
a 
helpful 
measure 
in 
improving 
everyday 
practice 
in 
rural 
areas. 
Water distribution 
systems 
are 
in 
extremely 
poor condition 
at 
both 
the 
municipal 
and 
rural 
levels. 
Users 
complain 
of 
lack 
of 
pressures, 
poor 
quality 
and 
irregularity 
in 
services. 
Water 
that 
was 
uncontaminated 
at 
the 
source 
often 
is 
contaminated 
in 
route 
to 
the 
consumer, 
and  water 
losses 
from 
leaks 
are 
considerable. 
About 
83% 
of 
those 
surveyed 
in 
rural 
areas 
depended 
on 
wells or 
springs 
for 
the 
water, 
and 
only 
about 
4% 
had 
access 
to 
piped 
water. 
Of 
those 
having 
access 
to 
piped 
water, 
only 
12% 
had 
water continuously and 
about 
half 
stated 
that 
there was never any water. 
Of 
those 
lacking 
access 
to 
piped 
water, about 
60% 
used 
well 
water 
exclusively 
and 
only 
about 
30% 
ever 
purchased 
bottled 
water. 
About 
54% 
felt 
the 
local 
mayor's 
office role 
in 
improving 
the 
supply 
of 
potable water 
was 
unsatisfactory 
or 
very 
unsatisfactory. 
About 
50% 
felt 
that water 
user 
associations 
or local 
village 
enterprises 
should 
handle 
responsibilities 
for 
investment 
in 
local 
water 
systems. 
Most 
users 
already 
pay 
some fees 
or 
purchase 
water 
commercially and 
would 
be 
willing 
to 
pay more 
for 
potable 
water and 
reliable 
service. 
In 
rural 
areas, 
for 
example, about 36% 
of 
those 
surveyed 
were 
paying 
for or 
had 
paid 
in 
the past 
for 
water, 
but 
about 
80% 
said 
they 
would 
be 
willing 
to 
pay 
for 
potable water 
in 
the 
future 
(25-30 
lei 
a month)  and 
even 
support 
rehabilitation 
costs. 
Similar 
responses 
were 
obtained 
from 
respondents 
in 
the 
municipalities.  The indicated 
ability 
and 
willingness 
to pay 
would 
cover cost 
recovery 
for 
the 
operational and 
maintenance 
costs necessary 
for 
the 
sustainability 
of 
the 
rehabilitated 
systems. 
Local 
specialists 
did 
not 
feel 
that 
the 
proposed 
approach 
would 
place any 
significant 
group 
at 
risk. 
They 
doubted that disconnections 
would 
result, 
rather 
suggesting 
that 
the 
poor 
would 
conserve 
their 
use 
of 
water 
more 
judiciously. 
This 
issue 
must 
be 
addressed 
with 
the 
specific  context 
of 
each 
locality 
with 
possible options 
for 
subsidization 
or 
solutions alternately 
acceptable 
to 
the 
community. 
It 
would 
be 
reviewed 
by 
the  respective 
water 
user 
organization 
for 
the 
sub-projects 
and 
monitored 
by 
the 
coordinating 
and 
monitoring 
NGO. 
The 
project 
seeks 
to 
balance 
the 
impact 
on rural 
and 
municipal 
populations, 
but 
is 
especially 
concerned 
with 
the 
impact 
on 
the 
poorest 
segments 
of 
society. 
At 
this 
level, 
women 
and 
children 
are 
often 
burdened 
with 
water-gathering 
tasks 
and 
are 
also 
especially vulnerable 
to 
health 
issues. 
The 
Social 
Assessment 
has 
assisted 
fine-tuning 
the 
implementation 
process 
and targeting 
the 
most vulnerable communities; 
the 
NGO 
responsible 
for 
the 
Social 
Assessment was 
especially sensitive 
to 
these 
issues. 
The 
lessons 
learned 
from 
the 
pilot 
sub-projects 
would 
serve 
as 
models 
for 
subsequent 
investment. 
6.2 
Participatory 
Approach: 
How 
are 
key 
stakeholders 
participating 
in 
the 
project? 
The 
rural 
assessment 
incorporates 
both 
standard 
household 
surveys 
and 
PRA 
methodology. 
A 
demonstration 
of 
PRA 
methodology 
was 
provided 
to 
the 
local 
NGO 
as 
a 
capacity-building 
exercise 
in 
advance 
of 
the 
commencement 
of 
the 
assessment. 
This 
methodology 
enables 
enhanced 
access 
to 
user 
perspectives, 
both 
in 
terms 
of 
immediacy 
and 
lower 
costs. 
The application 
of 
the 
methodology 
to 
the 
appraisal 
of 
other 
issues 
and 
projects 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
an 
indirect 
benefit 
for 
the 
assessment 
process. 
The 
Social 
Assessment 
has 
also 
helped 
to 
identify 
additional 
key 
stakeholders 
and 
the 
extent 
to 
which 
their 
participation 
in 
the 
project 
is 
desirable. 
MECTD, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
are 
the 
three 
major 
Government 
stakeholders. 
The 
Apa 
Canals 
and 
NGOs 
serving 
as 
water 
- 
32 
- 
Page  37
user  associations 
in 
municipalities 
constitute 
the 
primary 
stakeholders 
and 
have  assisted 
in 
the 
identification 
of 
other 
groups 
key 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project. They 
have also 
participated 
in 
the 
focus groups 
of 
the 
municipal 
Social 
Assessment 
that 
provided 
key 
input 
into 
the 
National 
Water 
Plan. 
A 
major 
collaborator 
in 
the 
sector 
is 
the 
Social 
Investment 
Fund, 
but 
other 
Donors 
such 
as 
Denmark 
represent stakeholders 
as 
well.  Consultation 
with 
both 
these 
groups 
indicated 
that 
the 
project 
as 
designed 
was 
neither 
duplicative 
nor 
competitive 
with 
the 
activities 
of 
these 
groups. 
The 
role 
of 
the 
Bank 
as 
a 
coordinator 
for Donor 
investment 
in 
the 
sector 
would 
ensure 
continued 
complementarity. 
Creating a 
more collaborative 
environment 
among stakeholders 
is 
seen 
as 
one 
of 
the 
objectives 
of 
the 
participatory 
approach 
being 
used, 
and 
it 
would 
serve 
as 
a 
model 
for 
application 
by 
the 
Govemment. 
During 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project, 
water 
user 
associations and 
NGOs 
would 
assist 
in 
the 
monitoring 
process 
discussed 
below 
increasing 
cooperative 
links 
between 
them 
and 
the Govemment. 
Privatization 
plans 
for 
the 
municipalities 
would 
focus 
on 
outsourcing 
for 
specialized 
services 
on 
a 
selective 
basis. 
Possible 
options 
for 
such 
outsourcing 
were  discussed 
with 
managers 
during 
the 
two 
preparatory workshops 
held 
with 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
interested 
in 
participating 
in 
the project. 
The 
staff 
currently 
providing 
such  support 
would 
be 
transferred 
to 
private 
firms 
under 
employment 
release 
packages 
that 
correspond 
to 
the 
provisions 
of 
national labor 
legislation. 
These  packages 
should 
adequately 
cover 
the 
transition 
process, 
especially 
because 
the service 
contract under 
which 
they 
would 
be 
hired 
would 
be immediately 
effective. 
Such 
staff 
would 
represent 
project 
beneficiaries 
in 
that they 
should benefit 
from 
increased 
remuneration 
as 
a result 
of 
the 
more 
efficient 
operation 
of 
private 
services. 
The works 
foreseen 
do not require 
new 
construction 
but 
are 
mainly 
re-fittings 
in 
existing 
structures 
on 
lands 
already 
considered 
municipal 
or 
village  property 
or 
for which 
the 
municipality 
or 
village 
has 
right-of-way 
assignments. 
As 
indicated 
in 
the 
EMP, 
there 
are 
no 
issues 
related 
to 
resettlement. 
All 
contracts 
providing for 
the 
procurement 
of 
works 
would 
include 
clauses 
requiring 
the development 
of 
mitigation 
plans 
should 
any such 
problems 
be 
identified, 
including illegal 
settlement. 
6.3 
How 
does 
the 
project 
involve 
consultations 
or 
collaboration 
with 
NGOs or 
other 
civil 
society 
organizations? 
At 
both 
the 
municipal level but 
especially 
at 
the 
village 
level,  the 
project 
would 
involve 
Water 
User 
Associations, 
NGOs 
and 
local 
community 
organizations 
in 
project 
implementation. 
Consultation 
with 
such  groups 
has 
already  been 
used 
to 
(i) 
represent 
user 
perspectives 
and 
(ii) 
identify 
investment 
priorities. 
They 
would play 
an 
especially 
important 
role 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
investments 
in 
the 
villages 
to 
be 
included 
under 
the 
project. 
During 
implementation, 
they 
would 
assist 
in 
the 
public 
education 
campaign, 
using 
a 
variety 
of 
media  and 
other 
means 
such 
as 
schools, 
health 
clinics 
and 
newspapers. 
They 
would 
have 
a 
role 
in 
monitoring 
as 
well. 
Working 
with 
the 
national 
office 
of 
such 
groups that have 
local 
offices 
would 
further 
enhance 
the 
project's 
outreach 
to 
rural 
areas 
in 
the 
interior. 
6.4 
What institutional 
arrangements 
have 
been 
provided to 
ensure 
the 
project 
achieves 
its 
social 
development outcomes? 
- 
33 
- 
Page  38
Building 
upon 
the 
sector 
reforms 
proposed 
in 
the 
draft 
National 
Water 
Action 
Plan 
and 
the 
stated 
priorities 
of 
the three 
key 
client 
ministries 
(MECTD, 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture) 
for 
improved 
access 
to 
safer 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
services 
for 
the 
population 
living 
in 
medium-sized 
towns and 
rural 
areas, 
the 
project 
has 
high 
Government 
ownership. 
This 
is 
especially 
important 
to 
the 
promotion 
of 
improved 
inter-ministerial 
collaboration, 
which 
is 
essential 
to 
the 
achievement 
of 
the 
social 
development 
outcomes. 
The 
project 
would 
advise 
and recommend 
policies 
and 
procedures 
to 
ensure 
such 
collaboration. 
The 
World 
Bank 
has 
also 
been 
asked 
to 
establish 
a 
Donor 
Coordination 
Task 
Force 
and 
take 
the 
lead 
in 
Donor 
coordination 
for 
the 
sector. 
Joint 
collaboration 
with 
the 
Social Investment 
Fund 
would 
also 
maximize project impact and 
minimize 
duplication 
of 
efforts and 
investments. 
Institutional 
strengthening 
would 
be 
provided not 
only 
at 
the 
ministerial 
level, 
but 
also 
to 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
and 
their 
new 
owners, 
the

and 
user associations 
in

and

At 
the 
municipal 
level 
the 
project 
would 
promote 
the 
development 
of 
a user-oriented 
culture 
on 
the 
part 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals. 
This 
would 
include 
the 
preparation 
of 
an 
Action 
Plan 
and the 
institution 
of 
a 
Customer 
Service 
Office 
staffed 
by 
at 
least 
two 
persons. 
The 
current 
focus concerns 
collection; 
satisfactory 
service 
needs 
to 
be 
incorporated 
into 
the 
orientation 
of 
the 
staff 
of 
water 
providers. 
The objective 
of 
the 
office 
would 
be 
to 
facilitate 
communication 
with 
the 
consumers, 
monitor 
and analyze 
the 
issues 
and 
concerns 
raised 
by 
consumers and develop 
a 
problem-solving 
capacity 
within 
each 
Apa 
Canal. 
At

village 
level, 
institutional 
strengthening 
would 
focus 
on 
the departments 
responsible 
for 
water and 
sewerage 
services 
under

office 
of 
the 
mayor 
in 
rural

and 
upon 
local 
user 
and

A 
joint

Water 
Working-Group 
would 
link 
these 
agents 
to 
the

in

Lessons

mission

implementation 
to 
indicate 
outcomes and 
user 
satisfaction. 
Focus 
groups 
would 
be 
the 
primary

instrument. 
The 
monitoring 
process 
would 
take 
place 
annually and 
if 
budget 
permits, 
then 
biannually.

20

in 
Europe. 
The

is 
the 
primary 
watercourse 
flowing 
into 
the 
Black 
Sea. 
Cahul 
is 
the 
6th 
largest 
town 
in 
Moldova 
with 
a 
population 
of 
about 
44,000.

the

about

entities/consumers.

Annex 
4: 
Cost Effectiveness 
Analysis 
Summary 
MOLDOVA: 
PILOT 
WATER SUPPLY 
AND 
SANITATION 
PROJECT 
Economic 
Justification 
of 
Project 
Background 
The 
associated 
benefits 
from 
each 
of 
the 
four 
components 
differ 
and the economic 
justification 
would 
differ 
accordingly. 
The 
investments 
to 
raise 
efficiency 
can 
be 
shown 
to 
produce reductions 
in 
those 
investment 
and 
operating 
costs 
that 
are necessary 
to 
provide 
the 
current 
level 
of 
service 
and 
expand 
capacity 
to 
meet future 
demand. 
Because 
both 
benefits 
and 
costs 
from 
this 
component 
are 
possible 
to 
quantify,  economic 
cost-benefit  analysis 
has 
been 
selected 
for 
this 
component that 
represents  56% 
of 
total 
project 
costs. 
Summary of 
benefits 
and 
costs: 
The 
water 
supply 
and 
wastewater 
systems 
in 
Moldova 
were 
designed 
and 
built 
with 
the 
parameters 
and 
workmanship 
that characterized 
the 
former 
Soviet 
Union. 
Moldova 
was one 
of 
the 
15 
Soviet 
Republics, 
followed 
state 
design 
practices 
and 
used 
domestic 
manufactured 
material. 
The 
systemic 
under-investment 
in 
maintenance 
worsened  the 
situation, 
as 
did 
the 
lack 
of 
resources 
in 
the 
decade 
subsequent 
to 
independence 
in 
1991. 
The 
result 
is 
a 
seriously 
deteriorated 
infrastructure. 
Past 
severely 
distorted 
input 
prices 
are 
approaching prices that reflect true scarcity 
in 
a 
market 
economy. 
In 
particular, electric 
energy 
prices 
are 
now 
rapidly 
rising, 
following 
the 
entry 
of 
a 
Spanish 
utility, 
Union 
Fenosa, 
into 
the 
national 
electric 
power 
sector. 
Prices have 
trebled 
within 
a 
few 
years 
to 
a 
level 
of 
US$0.054 
per 
kwh. 
The 
price 
increase 
has 
had 
a 
strong 
impact 
on 
water 
utility 
costs 
and 
electric 
energy, 
which 
now 
constitute 
from 
30% 
to 
60% 
of 
total 
operating 
costs. 
Under 
this 
difficult 
adjustment, 
the 
project 
economic 
priority 
is 
to 
correct 
for 
deferred 
maintenance 
and  rehabilitation. 
Summarizing, 
the 
project’s 
maintenance, 
equipment 
replacement, 
leak 
detection 
and 
demand 
management 
through metering 
could be 
expected 
to 
produce 
high 
returns 
as 
shown 
in 
the table below: 
Table: 
Illustrative 
Rates-of-Return 
on 
the Project 
Investments

of

Main 
Assumptions: 
Economic Rate-of-Return 
on 
Maintenance 
Investments 
The 
economic 
cost 
benefit 
of 
bringing 
pipe 
maintenance 
up 
to 
the 
best 
practice levels 
of 
2% 
is 
illustrated 
by 
the 
following 
calculation. 
An 
annual 
maintenance 
level 
of 
2% 
of 
the 
replacement 
value 
of 
assets 
can 
increase 
the 
useful 
life 
per 
unit 
of 
pipe 
from 
10 
years 
(without 
maintenance)  to, 
say, 
50 
years 
(with 
good 
maintenance). 
The 
investment 
in 
maintenance 
would 
have 
an 
economic rate 
of 
return 
of 
33% 
In 
reality, 
this 
rate 
of 
return 
underestimates 
actual economic 
rates 
of 
return 
because 
deteriorated 
pipe 
networks 
increase 
friction 
losses 
and 
water 
losses. 
- 
46 
- 
Page  51
Economic 
Rate-of-Return on 
Pump 
Replacement 
Some 
of 
the 
individual 
project 
cities 
have 
pumping 
equipment  intended 
to 
pump 
large 
quantities 
of 
irrigation 
water 
but 
instead 
are 
used 
to 
pump 
potable water 
within 
the 
cities. 
The 
consequence 
is 
that 
they 
are 
grossly 
over 
designed 
and 
are 
operated 
a 
few 
hours 
per 
day 
after 
which 
they 
are shut 
down 
for 
lack 
of 
storage 
capacity 
to 
accommodate 
the 
excessive 
pumping 
rates. 
The 
result 
is 
low 
efficiency 
in 
pumping, 
relatively 
higher 
electric 
energy 
costs 
and possibly intermittent water 
service 
for 
consumers. 
Replacing 
the 
pump 
would 
decrease 
energy consumption 
since 
a 
more 
efficient 
pump 
could 
raise 
the 
pump 
efficiency 
from 
an 
estimated 
value 
of 
70% 
to 
90% 
which 
is 
the 
expected 
level 
for 
the 
new 
and 
properly 
operated 
pumps. 
Assuming 
a 
useful 
life 
of 
a 
new 
pump 
of 
15 years, 
an 
investment 
cost 
of 
US$25,000 
for 
a 
100 
HP 
pump, 
electric 
energy 
costs 
of 
USSO.O54/kWh, 
and 
pumping 
with 
the 
full 
effect 
12 
hours 
per day 
the 
resulting 
rate-of-return 
for an 
investment 
in 
pump 
replacement 
would 
be 
27% 
since 
the investment 
for 
a 
new 
pump 
would 
be recovered after 
about 
five 
years 
by 
the 
decreased 
energy 
bills 
of 
US$5,500. 
Economic 
Rate-of-Return 
on 
Leakage 
Control and 
Metering 
The condition 
of 
existing 
pipe 
is 
poor, 
as 
evidenced 
by 
pipe 
burst 
in 
the order 
of 
1.3 
bursts 
per 
year 
and 
kilometer 
as 
compared 
to 
rates 
of 
0.2 
burst 
per 
year 
and kilometer 
in 
well 
managed 
utilities. 
Similarly, 
leakage 
rates 
per 
meter 
of 
the 
distribution 
system 
have been 
found 
to 
be 
up 
to 
60 
times 
those 
found 
in 
well-controlled 
international 
distribution 
systems. 
The 
consequence 
is 
high 
supply 
rates 
to 
meet 
reasonable 
consumption 
levels and the presently 
excessive leakage. 
For 
instance, 
in 
certain 
areas 
of 
the 
project 
city 
Cahul 
water supplied 
was 
found 
to be 
around 
520 
lcd 
of 
domestic 
consumption. 
Such 
a 
level 
should 
be 
compared 
to 
German 
domestic 
consumption 
in 
the 
order 
of 
120 lcd,  equivalent 
to 
per 
capita 
supplies 
of 
150 
lcd 
(assuming 
unaccounted 
water of 
20%). 
The 
combined effect 
of 
reduced income 
levels and better 
management 
of 
demand 
could be 
expected 
to 
reduce 
the 
present 
excessive 
supply 
levels 
substantially. 
Studies 
in 
other 
countries 
have 
shown 
that 
the 
reduction 
in 
consumption 
from 
individual 
metering 
alone 
could 
be 
expected 
to 
be 
in 
the order 
of 
40%. 
Assuming 
on 
average 
5.6 
persons 
per 
service 
connection 
(as 
is 
the 
case 
for 
the 
project 
city 
of 
Cahul), 
an 
individual 
water 
meter 
could 
then 
be 
expected 
to 
reduce 
the 
daily 
consumption 
by 
1,160 
liters 
per 
day 
or 
by 
425 
m3 
per 
year. 
The 
annual 
cost 
of 
purchasing 
and reading 
a 
meter 
would 
be 
in 
the order 
of 
US$24. 
Assuming 
an 
annual incremental 
operating 
cost 
per 
m3 
of 
USSO. 
10, 
the economic rate 
of 
return 
of 
individual 
metering 
for 
those 
residential 
connections that 
lack 
it 
would 
be 
in 
the 
order 
of 
78%. 
Cost-effectiveness 
indicators: 
Cost Effectiveness 
of 
Rural 
Water 
Supply 
Component 
The 
second 
component 
to 
protect 
rural 
wells 
represents 
15% 
of total 
project 
costs. 
Rigorous 
economic 
cost-benefit analysis 
is 
an 
unsatisfactory 
methodology 
since 
the expected 
health 
benefits 
are 
exceedingly 
difficult 
to 
quantify. 
This 
component 
is 
still 
justified 
economically, however, 
because 
protection 
of 
rural 
shallow 
wells 
and 
sanitary 
education 
are 
arguably 
the  cost 
effective 
measure 
to 
safeguard 
the 
rural 
population 
from 
water 
and 
sanitation-related 
disease. 
The 
proposed  investment 
of 
USSl. 
1 
million 
amounts 
to 
US$20 
per 
rural 
dweller 
expected 
to 
benefit 
from 
expenditure 
under 
this 
component. 
The 
proposed 
per 
capita expenditure 
could be 
compared 
to 
the 
range 
of 
rural 
water 
supply 
investments 
per 
capita 
of 
USS120 
for 
the 
new 
Borceag 
system 
financed 
by 
DANCEE 
and 
to 
the 
US$13 
for 
the 
15 
systems 
financed 
by 
the 
Moldova 
Social 
Investment 
Fund. 
The 
activities 
to 
improve 
the 
health 
of 
the 
rural 
population 
who rely 
on 
simple 
shallow 
wells 
are 
also 
justified 
because 
of 
the 
social benefits 
of 
supporting this 
population 
segment 
which 
comprises 
those 
with 
the 
lowest income 
in 
Moldova. 
- 
47 
- 
Page  52
Economic 
Justification 
of 
Remaining 
Components 
The 
economic 
justification 
of 
investments 
and 
expenditure 
to 
strengthen 
sector 
institutions 
(US$1.8 
million, or 
18% 
of 
total project 
costs) 
and 
for 
advisory and 
consulting 
services 
(US$l 
.O 
million, 
or 
10% 
of 
total 
project 
costs) 
do 
not 
lend 
themselves 
well 
for 
either 
economic  cost-benefit 
analysis 
or 
cost-effectiveness  analysis. 
These  costs  are 
necessary 
for 
the 
sector 
reforms  that 
are 
expected 
to 
strengthen 
the 
capacity 
of 
the 
country 
to 
prepare, 
implement 
and 
operate 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
systems. 
Such 
reforms 
would 
yield 
long-term 
economic 
and 
social 
benefits, 
the 
value 
of 
which 
should 
well 
exceed 
the 
modest 
expenditure 
of 
US$2.8 
million 
for 
these 
two 
components.

Annex 
6(A): 
Procurement Arrangements 
MOLDOVA: 
PILOT 
WATER 
SUPPLY 
AND 
SANITATION PROJECT 
Procurement 
Civil 
works, 
goods 
and 
services 
to 
be 
financed 
by 
the 
Bank 
would 
be procured 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
"Guidelines 
for 
Procurement 
under 
IBRD 
Loans and 
IDA 
Credits" 
published 
by 
the 
Bank 
in 
January 
1995 
and 
revised 
in 
January and 
August 
1996, 
September 1997 
and 
January  1999 
and "Guidelines 
for 
Selection and 
Employment 
of 
Consultants 
by 
World 
Bank 
Borrowers" 
published 
in 
January 1997 
and 
revised 
in 
September 
1997, 
January 1999 
and 
May 
2002. 
The 
project 
elements, 
their 
estimated cost 
and 
procurement 
methods 
are 
summarized 
in 
Tables 
A 
and 
AI. 
The 
thresholds 
for 
each 
procurement 
method 
and 
Bank 
prior 
review 
are 
shown 
in 
Table 
B. 
A 
procurement 
plan 
detailing 
the 
packaging and 
estimated 
schedule 
of 
the 
major 
procurement actions 
is 
also enclosed 
in 
Table 
B1. 
For 
purposes 
of 
convenience, 
procurement  contracts  have 
been 
shown 
separately 
for 
each 
project 
component. 
The 
total 
value 
of 
Bank-financed 
goods, 
works 
and services 
procurement 
is 
estimated 
at 
US$12.0 
million 
out 
of 
a 
total 
project 
cost 
of 
US$14.0 
million. 
Approximately 
US$250,000 
of 
project 
cost 
has 
been 
allocated 
to 
cover 
the 
strengthening and 
operating 
costs 
of 
PIU. 
The 
PIU 
has 
already 
been  established 
under 
the 
MECTD 
for 
project 
implementation, 
including 
procurement 
and 
financial 
management 
aspects 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
PIU 
is 
currently 
in 
the 
process 
of 
being 
staffed 
with 
qualified 
personnel. 
A 
Project Coordinator,  Procurement 
Head 
and Accountant 
has 
already 
been 
hired. 
A 
Procurement 
Analyst 
would 
be 
hired 
before 
the 
project 
becomes 
effective 
to 
ensure 
that procurement 
is 
undertaken 
efficiently, 
in 
a 
timely 
manner, 
and 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Bank 
guidelines. 
The 
PIU 
would 
have 
overall 
responsibility 
for 
project 
implementation, 
including 
procurement 
actions. 
For 
purposes 
of 
convenience, 
procurement 
contracts have 
been 
shown 
separately 
for 
each 
project city, 
however, 
actual procurement 
of 
similar 
goods and 
works 
would 
be 
grouped 
into 
larger 
ICB 
packages. 
1. 
Procurement 
of 
Civil 
Works 
Civil 
Works 
would 
be 
procured 
as 
follows: 
(a) 
International 
Competitive 
Bidding 
(ICB): 
All 
works 
contracts 
estimated 
to 
cost 
US$200,000 
equivalent 
or 
over 
per 
contract 
would 
be procured 
through 
ICB. 
(b) 
National 
Competitive 
Bidding 
(NCB): 
Works 
contracts  estimated 
to 
cost 
less 
than 
US$200,000 
equivalent 
per 
contract 
may 
be 
procured through 
NCB. 
(c) 
Procurement 
of 
Minor 
Works 
(MW): 
Works 
estimated 
to 
cost 
less 
than 
US$50,000 
equivalent 
per 
contract 
up 
to 
an 
aggregate 
amount 
of 
USS800,OOO 
may 
be 
procured 
under 
contracts 
awarded 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
quotations  obtained 
from 
three 
qualified 
domestic 
contractors 
in 
response 
to 
a 
written 
invitation.  The 
invitation 
shall  include 
a 
detailed 
description 
of 
works, 
including 
basic  specifications, 
the 
required completion 
date, 
a basic 
form 
of 
agreement, 
and 
relevant 
drawings, 
where 
applicable. 
2. 
Procurement 
of 
Goods 
Goods 
would 
be 
procured 
in 
package 
sizes 
that 
would 
encourage 
International Competitive 
Bidding. 
- 
59 
- 
Page  64
(a) 
International 
Competitive 
Bidding 
(ICB): 
Goods contracts estimated 
to 
cost 
US$lOO,OOO 
equivalent 
or 
over 
per 
contract 
would 
be 
procured through 
ICB. 
(b) 
International 
Shopping 
(IS): 
Goods estimated 
to 
cost 
less 
than 
US$lOO,OOO 
equivalent 
per 
contract 
up 
to 
an 
aggregate 
amount 
of 
US$400,000 
may 
be 
procured through 
IS. 
(c) 
National 
Shopping 
(NS): 
Goods  estimated 
to 
cost  less 
than 
US$50,000 
equivalent 
per 
contract 
up 
to 
an 
aggregate 
amount 
of 
US$550,000 
may 
be 
procured through 
NS. 
3. 
Selection 
Procedures 
for 
Consulting 
Services 
Contracts 
for 
Consulting 
Services 
would 
be 
awarded 
following 
the 
World 
Bank 
Guidelines "Selection 
and 
Employment 
of 
Consultants 
by 
World 
Bank 
Borrowers" 
dated January 
1997, 
revised 
September 
1997, 
January 1999 
and 
May 
2002. 
The 
following 
methods 
of 
selection 
would 
be 
followed: 
(a) 
Quality 
and 
Cost Based 
Selection 
(QCBS) 
would 
be 
the 
preferred method 
for 
selection 
of 
firms 
in 
contracts 
with 
estimated values above 
USS100,OOO. 
(b) 
Least 
Cost 
Selection 
(LCS) 
procedures 
is 
expected 
to 
be applied 
to 
selection 
of 
firms 
for 
audit 
services and 
similar 
assignments 
to 
cost 
less 
than 
US$lOO,OOO 
equivalent 
per 
contract. 
(c) 
Selection 
based 
on 
Consultants  Qualifications 
(CQ) 
would 
be 
used 
for 
very 
small 
consultant 
assignments 
to 
cost 
less 
than 
US$ 
100,000 
equivalent 
per 
contract. 
(d) 
Individual 
Consultant 
selection 
(ICs) 
procedures 
would 
be 
used 
for 
assignments 
which 
meet 
the requirements 
set 
forth 
in 
Section 
V 
of 
the 
Consultant Guidelines. 
(e) 
Single Source 
Selection 
procedure 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
applied 
to 
the 
selection 
of 
the 
firm 
for 
a 
contract 
estimated 
to 
cost 
around 
US$lOO,OOO 
equivalent 
to 
conduct the 
National 
Water 
Action 
Plan 
due 
to 
the 
fact that 
the 
work 
requires 
a 
continuation 
and 
institutional  memory 
that 
would 
be 
impossible 
to 
attain 
with 
a 
new 
firm. 
A 
local 
Association 
has 
undertaken 
comprehensive 
monitoring 
of 
performance indicators 
covering 
42 
providers 
of 
water 
supply 
and sanitation 
services 
from 
1996 
to 
the present. 
This 
process 
would 
need 
to 
be continued 
during 
project implementation along 
with 
the 
Donor 
Coordination 
Task Force 
to 
improve 
sector  knowledge, 
identify 
staffing 
and 
other 
institutional 
constraints, 
review 
operating 
practices and 
prepare a phased 
and 
prioritized long 
term 
sector 
investment program. 
In 
accordance 
with 
paragraph 
2.7 
of 
the 
Consultant 
Guidelines, 
short 
lists 
for 
consulting 
assignments 
may 
comprise 
entirely 
national 
consultants 
if 
the 
estimated contract amount 
is 
below 
US$ 
100,000.

of

of 
wide 
circulation. 
The 
Invitation 
to

and 
goods 
contracts 
would 
be

per

of 
the

Guidelines.

Annex 6(B): Financial Management and Disbursement 
Arrangements 
MOLDOVA: 
PILOT 
WATER 
SUPPLY 
AND 
SANITATION PROJECT 
Financial 
ManaPement 
1. 
Summary 
of 
the 
Financial 
Management 
Assessment 
Country 
Issues 
The 
current 
Country 
Financial Accountability 
Assessment 
(CFAA)  for 
Moldova 
as 
of 
September 
26, 
1995 
was 
prepared 
at 
a 
time 
of 
considerable 
transition 
and 
therefore, 
in 
view 
of 
the 
time 
that 
has 
since 
elapsed, 
is 
considered 
to 
be 
out-of-date. 
A 
revised 
CFAA 
is 
under 
preparation. The 
I-PRSP 
of 
November 
15, 
2000 
confirms 
that improvement 
is 
required 
in 
the 
management 
of 
public 
expenditures, 
including 
the 
budget 
process 
and 
budget 
execution, 
as 
well 
as 
cash 
and 
debt management. Thus 
the 
PIU 
has 
developed 
policies 
and 
procedures 
that 
operate 
in 
addition 
to 
those 
of 
the 
current 
public 
expenditure 
management 
framework 
to 
minimize 
project financial 
management 
risks. 
The 
Bank 
conducted a 
Country 
Portfolio 
Financial 
Management 
Review 
(CPFMR) 
of 
all 
projects 
under 
implementation 
in 
Moldova 
and 
identified 
some 
common 
financial 
management 
issues; 
the 
Bank 
confirmed 
that 
all 
such 
issues 
have 
been 
appropriately 
addressed 
in 
the 
design 
of 
the 
Pilot 
Water  Supply 
and  Sanitation  Project’s 
financial 
management arrangements. 
The banking 
sector 
in 
Moldova 
is 
relatively 
weak. 
However, the 
PIU 
has 
been 
operating 
satisfactorily 
its 
Policy 
and 
Human 
Resources 
Development (PHRD)  preparatory grant 
Special 
Account 
in 
a commercial bank 
acceptable 
to 
the 
Bank 
whose 
financial 
status 
and 
statements are 
reviewed 
on 
an 
ongoing 
basis 
by 
the 
Bank. 
As 
these 
arrangements have been satisfactory, 
they 
will 
remain 
in 
place 
during 
the 
Pilot 
Water 
Supply 
and Sanitation 
project 
implementation. 
Strengths 
and 
weaknesses 
The significant 
strengths 
that 
provide 
a 
basis 
of 
reliance 
on 
the 
project 
financial 
management 
system 
include: 
(i) 
the experience 
of 
the 
PIU 
Financial 
Manager 
in 
implementing Bank-financed projects and 
satisfying 
Bank financial 
management requirements; 
and 
(ii) 
PIU 
ring 
fencing 
(centralized)  arrangements 
and 
simple 
funds 
flow. 
There 
are 
no 
significant 
weaknesses 
of 
the 
project 
financial 
management 
system. 
Implementing 
Entity 
The 
PIU 
was established 
in 
the 
year 
2001 
as 
an 
implementing 
unit, 
created 
specifically 
to 
implement 
the 
Pilot 
Water Supply and Sanitation Bank-financed 
project.  The 
PIU 
reports to 
MECTD 
and 
MOF. 
The 
Apa 
Canals 
involved 
in 
the 
project 
are 
in 
an 
early 
development 
stage 
in 
the 
transition 
to 
the 
principles 
of 
commercial 
companies 
operating 
based 
on 
the 
market economy 
principles. 
Their financial 
management 
capabilities 
are 
limited 
and 
it 
was 
considered that 
these 
Apa 
Canals 
are 
not 
yet 
in 
a 
position 
to 
manage 
the 
project 
funds 
directly, 
hence the 
PIU 
be 
responsible 
and 
have 
all 
fiduciary 
aspects 
centralized. 
To 
this 
end, 
there 
are 
project 
funds 
set 
aside 
to 
undertake 
a 
significant 
strengthening 
of 
the 
entities 
financial 
management 
systems 
(accounting, 
reporting, 
cost 
recovery 
and 
monitoring 
systems). 
Flow 
of 
Funds 
Project 
funds 
will 
flow 
from: 
(i) 
the 
Bank, 
either 
via 
a 
single 
Special 
Account 
which 
will 
be 
replenished 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
SOEs 
or 
by 
direct payment 
on 
the basis 
of 
direct 
payment 
withdrawal 
applications; 
and 
(ii) 
the Government, 
via 
the Treasury 
at 
the 
MOF 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
payment 
requests 
prepared 
by 
the 
PIU. 
- 
69 
- 
Page  74
In 
respect 
of 
the village 
water 
supply 
component, 
the 
PIU 
will 
execute 
the payments 
directly 
to 
the 
suppliers. 
This 
component 
involves 
water 
supply 
activities 
for 
the 
villages 
selected 
and 
funding 
will 
be 
provided 
by 
assistance 
that 
will 
be 
managed 
by 
the 
PIU 
in 
accordance 
with 
World 
Bank 
fiduciary 
procedures. 
The 
PIU 
will 
receive 
these 
funds 
in 
a 
separate 
project 
special account 
and 
no 
funds 
will 
be 
transferred 
to 
beneficiaries 
(village 
communities) 
as 
there 
is 
very 
limited 
financial 
management 
capacity 
at 
the 
village 
level. 
The 
Credit 
Agreement 
will 
be 
signed between 
the 
World 
Bank 
(IDA) 
and 
the Government 
of 
Moldova. 
The 
Government 
will 
authorize the 
MECTD, 
through 
the 
PIU, 
to 
use 
the 
credit 
proceeds. 
There 
will 
be 
a 
single 
SA, 
for 
the 
whole 
project 
implementation. 
Project 
Agreements 
will 
be 
signed, 
with 
each 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals 
involved 
in 
the project. 
The 
PIU 
will 
execute the payments 
on 
behalf 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canals, 
and 
of 
behalf 
of 
the villages 
selected, 
acting 
as 
a 
payment 
‘agent’. 
The 
Apa 
Canals 
will 
make their 
own 
payments 
in 
respect 
of 
their 
contributions 
to 
the 
project 
from 
their 
current 
accounts 
and 
may 
also 
contribute 
in 
kind 
(labor) 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
the project. 
Staffing 
The 
PIU 
includes 
a Head,  a 
finance team 
comprising the Financial 
Manager 
and 
an 
Accountant 
and 
a 
Procurement  Specialist. 
The 
Financial 
Manager 
has 
good 
experience 
of 
implementing 
Bank-financed 
projects and 
has 
demonstrated that 
it 
is 
fully 
capable 
of 
fulfilling 
the accounting 
and reporting 
needs 
of 
the project. 
Accounting 
Policies and Procedures 
The 
accounting 
books and 
records 
are 
maintained 
on 
a 
cash basis 
and project 
financial 
statements 
are 
presented 
in 
Moldovan 
Lei. 
The 
PIU 
has 
instituted 
a 
set 
of 
appropriate 
accounting 
procedures 
and 
internal 
controls 
including 
authorization 
and 
segregation 
of 
duties, 
documented 
in 
the project financial 
management procedures manual. 
Internal 
Audit 
The 
PIU 
has 
no 
internal 
audit 
function 
and  none 
is 
considered 
necessary 
given 
the 
size 
of 
the 
organization. 
External 
Audit 
As 
the 
PIU 
is 
newly 
created, 
there 
is 
no 
track record regarding 
the external 
audit. 
The 
PIU 
has 
developed 
audit 
terms 
of 
reference  satisfactory 
to 
the  Bank, 
to 
include 
the  financial 
statements, 
SOEs 
and  Special 
Accounts, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
audits 
of 
the 
Apa 
Canal 
entities’ 
financial 
statements. 
The 
audit 
of 
the 
project 
will 
be 
conducted 
by 
independent 
private 
auditors 
acceptable 
to 
the 
Bank, 
on 
terms 
of 
reference 
acceptable 
to 
the Bank, 
and procured 
by 
the 
PIU 
through 
the 
Least-Cost 
Selection 
procurement 
process. 
The 
annual 
audited project 
financial 
statements 
will 
be 
provided to 
the 
Bank 
within 
six 
months 
of 
the 
end 
of 
each 
fiscal 
year 
and 
also 
at 
the 
closing 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
contract 
for 
the audit 
awarded 
during 
the 
first 
year 
of 
project implementation 
and 
thereafter 
may be 
extended 
from 
year-to-year 
with 
the 
same 
auditor,  subject 
to 
satisfactory performance. 
The 
costs 
of 
the audits 
will 
be 
financed 
from 
the 
proceeds 
of 
the 
IDA 
Credit.

Reporting and 
Monitoring 
The 
PIU 
produces 
all 
financial 
reports 
for 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
Government 
with 
its 
existing 
customized 
project 
software 
system, 
that 
it 
is 
able 
to report 
accurately and 
timely 
on 
project 
expenditures. 
Financial 
Monitoring 
Reports  (FMRs) 
will 
be 
used 
for 
project 
monitoring 
and 
supervision 
and 
the 
indicative 
formats 
of 
these are 
included 
in 
the 
project financial 
management procedures manual. 
The 
PIU 
will 
produce 
a 
full 
set 
of 
FMRs 
every 
calendar 
quarter throughout 
the 
life 
of 
the 
project. 
Information 
Systems 
The PIU’s 
information 
systems 
comprises 
its 
existing 
customized 
accounting, 
reporting and 
monitoring 
software, 
which 
is 
used 
for 
presenting 
all 
needed 
project 
financial 
reports, 
for both 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
Government, 
denominated 
in 
Lei. 
Supervision 
Plan 
During 
project 
implementation, 
the 
Bank 
will 
supervise 
the project’s 
financial 
management 
arrangements 
in 
two 
main 
ways: 
(i) 
review 
the 
project’s  quarterly 
financial 
management 
reports 
as 
well 
as 
the 
project’s 
and 
entities’  annual 
audited 
financial 
statements 
and 
auditor’s 
management letter; 
and 
(ii) 
during 
the 
Bank’s 
supervision 
missions, 
review 
the project’s 
financial 
management 
and 
disbursement 
arrangements 
(including 
a 
review 
of 
a 
sample 
of 
SOEs 
and 
movements 
on 
the Special Account) 
to 
ensure 
compliance 
with 
the 
Bank’s 
minimum 
requirements. 
As 
required, 
a 
Bank-accredited 
Financial 
Management 
Specialist 
will 
assist 
in 
the 
supervision 
process.

made 
available 
to 
IDA 
during 
supervision.

Special account: 
A 
Special 
Account 
will 
be 
opened at 
a 
commercial 
bank, acceptable 
to 
the 
World 
Bank. 
The 
minimum 
size 
of 
application 
for 
direct withdrawals and 
issuance 
of 
commitments 
from 
the 
IDA 
Credit 
account 
would 
be 
20 
percent 
of 
the amount 
of 
the 
Authorized 
Allocation 
to 
the Special Account. 
The Authorized Allocation 
for 
this 
special account 
would 
be 
US$l,OOO,OOO, 
representing 
about 
four 
months 
of 
average 
expenditures 
made 
through 
the 
Special 
Account. 
During 
the early 
stage 
of 
the 
project, 
the 
initial 
allocation 
to 
the 
Special 
Account 
would 
be limited 
to 
US$500,000. 
However, 
when 
the 
disbursements 
under 
the 
Credit 
have 
reached 
the 
level 
of 
SDR2,000,000-the 
initial 
allocation 
may be 
increased 
through 
an 
amendment 
to 
the 
Credit 
Agreement 
if 
the 
Borrower 
requests. 
Applications 
for 
replenishment 
of 
the 
Special 
Account 
would 
be 
submitted 
monthly 
(or earlier 
if 
desired 
by 
client), 
or 
quarterly 
at  the 
latest. 
Documentation 
requirements 
for 
replenishment 
would  follow 
standard 
Bank 
procedures 
as 
described 
in 
Disbursement Handbook, 
Chapter 
6. 
Monthly 
bank 
statements 
of 
the 
Special 
Account, 
which 
have  been 
reconciled 
by 
the  Borrower, 
would 
accompany 
all 
replenishment 
requests.

Additional 
Annex 
11 
: 
Environmental Assessment Summary 
MOLDOVA: 
PILOT 
WATER 
SUPPLY 
AND 
SANITATION PROJECT 
A. 
Background 
1. 
Project 
Objectives. 
The overall goal 
of 
the 
project 
is 
to 
enhance 
the 
welfare 
of 
the 
population 
living 
in 
small 
towns 
and 
medium-size 
cities 
by 
improving 
access 
to 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation 
services, 
The 
strategy 
is 
to 
assist 
in 
creating 
a 
framework 
that 
would 
enable 
the  implementation, 
sustainable 
management 
and 
regulation 
of 
essential 
water 
supply 
and 
sanitation infrastructure. 
The project 
would 
be 
implemented 
in 
areas 
with 
problems 
of 
water 
availability 
or 
water 
quality, 
a 
relatively 
large 
population 
of 
poor 
and socially 
disadvantaged 
people 
and 
high 
community 
demand 
for 
improved 
services. 
2. 
The 
investment 
program 
is 
to 
be 
financed 
by 
IDA 
under 
the 
proposed 
Moldova 
Pilot 
Water  Supply 
and Sanitation  Project. 
The 
proposed 
project 
is 
classified 
as 
Category 
B 
because 
its 
potential 
adverse 
environmental 
impacts 
on 
human 
populations 
or 
environmentally important 
areas-including 
wetlands, 
forests, 
grasslands, 
and other 
natural 
habitats-are 
not 
significant and 
are 
site-specific. 
Few 
if 
any 
of 
them 
are 
irreversible,  and 
in 
most 
cases 
mitigatory 
measures 
do 
not 
require 
a 
special 
study 
and 
can 
be 
designed 
quickly. 
Environmental 
Category. 
In 
accordance 
with 
World 
Bank 
Operational 
Policy 
4.01 
(January 
1999) 
the 
project 
is 
rated 
as 
Category 
B 
and 
requires 
the 
preparation 
an Environmental 
Assessment 
that includes 
an 
EMP. 
3. 
Investment 
Components 
High-priority 
investment 
(first 
year). 
This 
package consists 
of 
immediate-term options 
of 
production 
metering, 
backlog leak 
detection 
and 
repair, 
including 
the 
purchase 
of 
equipment 
and 
necessary 
training, 
metering 
of 
non-domestic 
and 
external 
metering 
of 
apartment 
blocks  and  private 
houses, 
energy 
efficiency 
study 
including 
pumps 
replacement, 
water 
treatment 
and 
wastewater 
treatment 
plants 
operation improvement, 
installation 
of 
multi-level electricity 
meters 
and 
network 
zoning. 
Medium 
term 
(years 
two-five). 
Based 
on 
Package 
1 
and 
adds 
prioritized 
mains 
replacement  and 
service 
connection 
and 
the 
ongoing pipeline 
repair 
and 
service 
connection 
repair. 
B. 
Environmental Management 
and 
Mitigation 
Plan 
4. 
The 
PIU 
together 
with 
the international  consultant prepared detailed 
plans 
for 
each 
Utility 
to 
monitor 
implementation of 
mitigating

updates the

Additional 
Annex 
12: 
Social 
Assessment Summary 
MOLDOVA 
PILOT 
WATER 
SUPPLY 
AND 
SANITATION PROJECT 
In 
conjunction 
with 
the 
preparation 
of 
the 
Pilot 
Water 
Supply 
and  Sanitation  Project,  a  Social 
Assessment 
of 
35 
rural 
communities 
was 
also conducted. 
Methodology. 
The 
Social 
Assessment 
was 
composed 
of 
several 
instruments: 
standard 
household 
survey 
using 
written 
forms, 
participatory 
focus 
groups, 
consultations 
with 
specialists 
and 
the 
collection 
of 
economic and 
social 
data 
from 
the 
public 
files 
of 
the 
local 
offices 
of 
the 
local 
governments. 
The 
size 
of 
the 
communities included 
in 
the 
assessment 
ranged 
from 
just 
under 
2,000 
to 
just 
over 
7,000. 
The 
communities  were 
selected 
on 
a geographically balanced 
basis 
including 
regional 
population 
density. 
The 
household 
survey 
included 
600 
respondents 
from 
23 
communities  and 
the  focus 
groups  were 
conducted 
in 
35 
communities, 
including 
the 
23 
communities 
also 
covered 
by 
the 
household 
survey. 
Consultations 
with 
specialists 
were 
held 
in 
19 
communities. 
Interviewers received 
special 
training 
and 
were 
provided 
a 
written 
manual 
for 
the 
conduct 
of 
the 
interview 
process. 
General 
results. 
The 
problem 
of 
the 
limited 
availability 
of 
potable 
water 
was 
cited 
as 
a 
serious 
concern 
by 
51% 
of 
all 
the 
people 
interviewed 
in 
the 
household 
survey. 
Half 
of 
the  focus  groups  gave the 
seriousness 
of 
this 
problem 
the 
highest 
rank 
of 
10. 
In 
general,  however, 
the 
respondents 
in 
these 
communities were 
not 
well 
informed 
about the 
quality 
of 
the water 
they 
consumed. 
Most 
realized 
the 
economic costs 
of 
water 
scarcity 
but 
the 
important 
link 
between 
health 
and water 
quality 
was 
not 
well 
recognized. 
Water 
supply 
among 
key 
rural 
problems. 
Half 
of 
the respondents 
in 
the 
household 
survey 
indicated 
that the 
need 
for 
potable water 
was 
the 
most 
serious 
problem 
faced 
by 
their 
community. 
The next 
two 
most 
important 
problems 
included 
the 
poor 
state 
of 
the roads 
and 
the 
need 
for 
access 
to 
natural 
gas. 
Only 
4.2% 
of 
the 
households surveyed 
had 
piped 
water 
system 
and 
the 
majority 
relied 
on 
wells 
and 
pumps 
(83%). 
About 
67% 
felt 
that 
the 
lack 
of 
adequate 
water  was 
a 
serious 
or 
very 
serious 
problem. 
The 
concern 
over 
this 
issue 
in 
the 
central 
and southern regions, 
reaching 
between 
80% 
and 
90% 
of 
those 
interviewed, 
was 
higher 
than 
in 
the 
northern 
region. 
Over 
half 
of 
the 
respondents were 
dissatisfied 
with 
both 
the 
lack 
of 
attention 
and 
the actions 
of 
their 
local 
administration relative 
to 
water 
issues. 
Awareness 
of 
water 
quality 
and 
impact 
on 
health. 
Respondents 
were 
not 
well 
informed 
about 
the 
quality 
of 
the water 
they 
consumed. 
Although 
more than 
32% 
of 
the households 
thought 
the 
water 
quality 
was 
not 
good, 
actual 
laboratory 
testing 
showed 
that 
70% 
to 
90% 
of 
all 
wells 
had 
excess 
levels 
of 
nitrates 
and  bacteria. 
The 
results 
varied 
geographically, 
however, 
with 
about 
two 
thirds 
of 
the 
respondents 
in 
the southern 
region 
of 
the 
country 
being 
more dissatisfied 
about 
water 
quality. 
In 
general, 
respondents 
did 
not 
comprehend 
the 
importance 
of 
poor quality 
water 
to 
their 
health. 
Only 
a 
third 
of 
the 
respondents 
saw 
a relationship 
between 
water 
quality 
and 
poor 
health, 
whereas 
health 
specialists 
cite 
the 
poor quality 
of 
water 
as 
the 
major 
environmental constraint 
on 
health. 
Sanitation 
problems. 
The 
respondents 
in 
the 
household 
survey 
indicated 
that 
local 
administrative 
authorities 
did 
very 
little, 
if 
anything, 
to 
address 
sanitation 
issues. 
This 
perspective 
was 
also 
confirmed 
in 
the focus group discussions. 
The 
major 
sources 
of 
pollution 
were 
garbage 
and 
animal 
waste. 
Most 
areas 
lacked 
designated 
areas 
for 
the 
proper 
disposal 
of 
household 
wastes. 
Inappropriately 
stocked 
animal 
wastes, 
intended 
for 
later 
use 
as 
fertilizer, 
were 
also sources 
of 
pollution 
to 
the 
water 
table. 
- 
83 
- 
Page  88
Level 
of 
water 
supply  and 
seriousness 
of 
sewerage 
problems. 
Only 
about 
half 
of 
the 
people 
understood 
the 
seriousness 
of 
sewage 
problems and 
the 
need 
for 
wastewater treatment. 
The 
provision 
of 
connections 
to 
piped 
water 
systems 
without 
adequate 
sewage 
treatment 
is 
a 
risk 
to 
further contamination 
of 
the water table. 
To 
limit 
this 
risk, 
specialists 
recommend 
providing 
water at 
points 
external 
to 
the 
households, 
limiting 
the 
total 
discharge 
of 
water 
from 
households 
until 
sewage 
treatment 
facilities 
can 
also 
be 
constructed. 
Models 
and 
mechanisms. 
The 
first 
priority 
given 
by 
respondents was 
the 
repair 
of 
existing 
wells 
and 
construction 
of 
new 
wells. 
The 
second need 
they 
identified 
was 
the 
construction 
of 
piped 
water 
systems. 
Experts, 
rather, 
felt 
that 
the 
quality 
of 
the water 
in 
shallow 
wells 
was 
often 
inferior 
and advised 
giving 
priority to 
investing 
in 
existing 
artesian 
wells 
or 
where 
necessary 
drilling 
new 
artesian wells. 
They 
also 
suggested 
targeting 
existing 
systems 
for 
investment. 
About 
80% 
of 
the 
households 
were 
willing 
to 
contribute 
to 
the 
cost 
of 
such 
projects. 
The 
focus  groups 
indicated 
that 
villagers 
were 
willing 
to 
contribute 
on 
a 
monthly 
basis 
for 
water 
services 
if 
there 
were 
meters 
installed 
(estimates 
ranged 
from 
25-30 
MDL). 
These discussions 
also 
indicated,  however, 
that 
some 
people 
still 
saw 
water 
as 
a 
“free 
good” 
for which 
they 
should 
not 
pay. 
Financing 
and 
improving 
water 
supply. 
The 
respondents 
realized 
that 
the 
resources 
of 
their 
local 
administrations were 
not 
enough 
to 
cover the cost 
of 
financing 
improvements 
in 
water 
supply, 
but 
most 
hoped 
that  grants 
could 
be obtained 
to 
cover 
the 
major 
cost 
of 
the  investment. 
Nearly 
half 
of 
the 
respondents 
felt 
that 
water 
user 
associations/NGOs 
should be  formed 
to 
manage  the 
investment. 
Discussions 
with 
local 
administrators indicated: 
(i) 
some 
concerns 
over 
this 
approach;  and 
(ii) 
the 
need 
for 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
in 
order 
for 
this 
approach 
to 
work. 
Conclusions. 
Although improving 
the 
supply 
of 
potable water 
is 
a 
problem 
for 
the 
entire country, 
the 
problem 
is 
most 
serious 
in 
the 
central 
and 
southern regions. 
Priorities 
should 
be 
focused 
on 
these 
areas: 
Rehabilitation 
and clean-up 
and 
of 
existing wells 
and 
elimination 
of 
near-by 
pollutants; 
Public 
awareness 
campaign for 
villagers 
concerning water and 
health 
issues; 
Create 
a dialogue 
between 
local 
authorities  and 
village 
communities 
to 
address 
the 
dissatisfaction 
with 
the 
activities relative 
to 
water 
issues; 
Villagers 
in 
general 
are 
ready 
to 
participate 
in 
some 
of 
the 
costs 
of 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
existing 
systems 
or 
construction 
of 
new 
or 
expanded 
systems 
as 
well 
as 
pay 
monthly 
fees 
provided 
there 
are 
meters. 
The 
degree 
of 
willingness 
to 
pay 
should 
be 
one 
of 
the 
criteria 
for 
the selection 
of 
villages 
for 
participation 
in 
assistance 
projects 
in 
general 
and 
the 
current proposed 
project 
in 
particular; 
In 
the 
case 
of 
debt 
cancellation, 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
Government  counter-investment 
in 
lieu 
of 
debt 
payment, 
priority 
should 
be 
given to 
investment 
in 
the 
water 
sector; 
and 
Establish 
user 
associations 
to 
manage 
and 
operate 
investment 
in 
the 
sector.
